     <p>
      <b>
       Birds
      </b>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">
       class
      </a>
      <b>
       Aves
      </b>
      ) are a group of
      <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">
       endothermic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">
       vertebrates
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Body_plan" title="Body plan">
       characterised
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">
       feathers
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">
       beak
      </a>
      with no
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teeth" title="Teeth">
       teeth
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Oviparity" title="Oviparity">
       laying
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Eggshell" title="Eggshell">
       hard-shelled
      </a>
      eggs, a high
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolic" title="Metabolic">
       metabolic
      </a>
      rate, a four-chambered
      <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">
       heart
      </a>
      , and a lightweight but strong
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bird_skeleton" title="Bird skeleton">
       skeleton
      </a>
      . Scientific consensus is that birds are an advanced subgroup of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theropod" title="Theropod">
       theropod
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">
       dinosaurs
      </a>
      . Birds have more or less developed
      <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">
       wings
      </a>
      ; the only known groups without wings are the
      <a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">
       moa
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Elephant_bird" title="Elephant bird">
       elephant birds
      </a>
      , which are generally considered to have became
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct" title="Extinct">
       extinct
      </a>
      in the 16th century. Wings are evolved
      <a href="/wiki/Forelimb" title="Forelimb">
       forelimbs
      </a>
      , and most bird species can
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_flight" title="Bird flight">
       fly
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Flightless_bird" title="Flightless bird">
       Flightless birds
      </a>
      include
      <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">
       ratites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">
       penguins
      </a>
      , and diverse
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemism_in_birds" title="Endemism in birds">
       endemic
      </a>
      island species. Some species of birds, particularly penguins and members of the
      <a href="/wiki/Anatidae" title="Anatidae">
       duck family
      </a>
      , are adapted for swimming. Birds also have
      <a href="/wiki/Digestion#Digestive_systems" title="Digestion">
       digestive
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">
       respiratory systems
      </a>
      that are uniquely adapted for flight. Some birds, especially
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corvids" title="Corvids">
       corvids
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parrots" title="Parrots">
       parrots
      </a>
      , are among the most
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intelligent_animal" title="Intelligent animal">
       intelligent animals
      </a>
      ; several bird species
      <a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals" title="Tool use by animals">
       make and use tools
      </a>
      , and many
      <a href="/wiki/Social_animal" title="Social animal">
       social species
      </a>
      pass on knowledge across generations, which is considered a form of culture.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many species annually
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">
       migrate
      </a>
      great distances. Birds are social, communicating with visual signals, calls, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bird_song" title="Bird song">
       songs
      </a>
      , and participating in such social behaviours as
      <a href="/wiki/Helpers_at_the_nest" title="Helpers at the nest">
       cooperative breeding
      </a>
      and hunting,
      <a href="/wiki/Flocking_(behavior)" title="Flocking (behavior)">
       flocking
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">
       mobbing
      </a>
      of predators. The vast majority of bird species are
      <a href="/wiki/Monogamy#Varieties_of_monogamy_in_biology" title="Monogamy">
       socially monogamous
      </a>
      , usually for one breeding season at a time, sometimes for years, but rarely for life. Other species have
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polygynous" title="Polygynous">
       polygynous
      </a>
      ("many females") or, rarely,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyandrous" title="Polyandrous">
       polyandrous
      </a>
      ("many males") breeding systems. Eggs are usually laid in a
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">
       nest
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation#Avian_incubation" title="Egg incubation">
       incubated
      </a>
      by the parents. Most birds have an extended period of parental care after hatching.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many species are economically important. Domesticated and undomesticated birds (
      <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">
       poultry
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_(food)" title="Game (food)">
       game
      </a>
      ) are important sources of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eggs_(food)" title="Eggs (food)">
       eggs
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">
       meat
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feathers" title="Feathers">
       feathers
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Songbird" title="Songbird">
       Songbirds
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Parrot" title="Parrot">
       parrots
      </a>
      , and other species are popular as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pets" title="Pets">
       pets
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">
       Guano
      </a>
      (bird excrement) is harvested for use as a
      <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">
       fertilizer
      </a>
      . Birds
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_fictional_birds" title="List of fictional birds">
       prominently figure
      </a>
      throughout human culture. About 120130 species have become extinct due to human activity since the 17th century, and hundreds more before then. Human activity threatens about 1,200 bird species with extinction, though efforts are underway to
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_conservation" title="Bird conservation">
       protect
      </a>
      them. Recreational
      <a href="/wiki/Birdwatching" title="Birdwatching">
       birdwatching
      </a>
      is an important part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">
       ecotourism
      </a>
      industry.
     </p>
     <p>
      Aves ranks as the
      <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">
       tetrapod
      </a>
      class with the most living species, approximately ten thousand (half of them being
      <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">
       passerines
      </a>
      ). Birds live worldwide and ranging in size from the 5cm (2in)
      <a href="/wiki/Bee_hummingbird" title="Bee hummingbird">
       bee hummingbird
      </a>
      to the 2.75m (9ft)
      <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">
       ostrich
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">
       fossil record
      </a>
      indicates that true birds first appeared during the
      <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">
       Cretaceous
      </a>
      period, around
      <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=100">
        100
       </a>
       million years ago.
      </span>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown2011_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-brown2011-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, primitive bird-like "stem-birds" that lie outside Aves proper, in the group
      <a href="/wiki/Avialae" title="Avialae">
       Avialae
      </a>
      , have been found dating back to the mid-
      <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">
       Jurassic
      </a>
      period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divergence_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-divergence-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many of these early stem-birds, such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx" title="Archaeopteryx">
        Archaeopteryx
       </a>
      </i>
      , were not yet capable of fully powered flight, and many retained primitive characteristics like toothy jaws in place of beaks and long bony tails.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divergence_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-divergence-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alonso_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Alonso-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_and_classification">
       Evolution and classification
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_birds" title="Evolution of birds">
       Evolution of birds
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Archaeopteryx_-_Eichst%C3%A4tt.jpg">
        <img alt=" Slab of stone with fossil bones and feather impressions" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1600" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Archaeopteryx_-_Eichst%C3%A4tt.jpg/220px-Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Archaeopteryx_-_Eichst%C3%A4tt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Archaeopteryx_-_Eichst%C3%A4tt.jpg/330px-Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Archaeopteryx_-_Eichst%C3%A4tt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Archaeopteryx_-_Eichst%C3%A4tt.jpg/440px-Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Archaeopteryx_-_Eichst%C3%A4tt.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naturkundemuseum_Berlin_-_Archaeopteryx_-_Eichst%C3%A4tt.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx_lithographica" title="Archaeopteryx lithographica">
          Archaeopteryx lithographica
         </a>
        </i>
        is often considered the oldest known true bird.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The first
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">
       classification
      </a>
      of birds was developed by
      <a href="/wiki/Francis_Willughby" title="Francis Willughby">
       Francis Willughby
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Ray" title="John Ray">
       John Ray
      </a>
      in their 1676 volume
      <i>
       Ornithologiae
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolus_Linnaeus" title="Carolus Linnaeus">
       Carolus Linnaeus
      </a>
      modified that work in 1758 to devise the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomic_classification" title="Taxonomic classification">
       taxonomic classification
      </a>
      system currently in use.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Birds are categorised as the
      <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">
       biological class
      </a>
      Aves in
      <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">
       Linnaean taxonomy
      </a>
      .
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_taxonomy" title="Phylogenetic taxonomy">
       Phylogenetic taxonomy
      </a>
      places Aves in the dinosaur
      <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">
       clade
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">
       Theropoda
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theropoda_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Theropoda-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">
       Definition
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Aves and a sister group, the clade
      <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">
       Crocodilia
      </a>
      , contain the only living representatives of the
      <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">
       reptile
      </a>
      clade
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archosauria" title="Archosauria">
       Archosauria
      </a>
      . During the late 1990s, Aves was most commonly defined
      <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">
       phylogenetically
      </a>
      as all descendants of the
      <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">
       most recent common ancestor
      </a>
      of modern birds and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx" title="Archaeopteryx">
        Archaeopteryx lithographica
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, an earlier definition proposed by
      <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Gauthier" title="Jacques Gauthier">
       Jacques Gauthier
      </a>
      gained wide currency in the 21st century, and is used by many scientists including adherents of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylocode" title="Phylocode">
       Phylocode
      </a>
      system. Gauthier defined Aves to include only the
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_group" title="Crown group">
       crown group
      </a>
      of the set of modern birds. This was done by excluding most groups known only from fossils, and assigning them, instead, to the Avialae,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in part to avoid the uncertainties about the placement of
      <i>
       Archaeopteryx
      </i>
      in relation to animals traditionally thought of as theropod dinosaurs.
     </p>
     <p>
      Gauthier
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gauthier.26dequeiroz2001_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gauthier.26dequeiroz2001-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      identified four conflicting ways of defining the term "Aves", which is a problem because the same biological name is being used four different ways. Gauthier proposed a solution, number 4 below, which is to reserve the term Aves only for the
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_group" title="Crown group">
       crown group
      </a>
      , the last common ancestor of all living birds and all of its descendants. He assigned other names to the other groups.
     </p>
     <table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; background-color: white; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 0em; width: 248px; float: right; clear: right; vertical-align: middle">
      <tr>
       <td style="border: 1px solid #dedede; padding: 3px">
        <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="font-size:75%">
         <tr>
          <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
           <br/>
          </td>
          <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
           <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="">
            <tr>
             <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
              <br/>
             </td>
             <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
              <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="">
               <tr>
                <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                 <br/>
                </td>
                <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                 <p>
                  <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">
                   Crocodiles
                  </a>
                 </p>
                </td>
               </tr>
               <tr>
                <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                 <br/>
                </td>
               </tr>
               <tr>
                <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                 <br/>
                </td>
                <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                 <p>
                  <b>
                   Birds
                  </b>
                 </p>
                </td>
               </tr>
               <tr>
                <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                 <br/>
                </td>
               </tr>
              </table>
             </td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
             <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">
              <br/>
             </td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
             <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
              <br/>
             </td>
             <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
              <p>
               <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">
                Turtles
               </a>
              </p>
             </td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
             <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
              <br/>
             </td>
            </tr>
           </table>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">
           <br/>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
           <br/>
          </td>
          <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
           <p>
            <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">
             Lizards
            </a>
            (including
            <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">
             Snakes
            </a>
            )
           </p>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
           <br/>
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; width: 248px; border: 0; padding: 0;">
        The birds' phylogenetic relationships to major living reptile groups.
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <ol>
      <li>
       Aves can mean those advanced
       <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">
        archosaurs
       </a>
       with feathers (alternately
       <a href="/wiki/Avifilopluma" title="Avifilopluma">
        Avifilopluma
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       Aves can mean those that fly (alternately Avialae)
      </li>
      <li>
       Aves can mean all reptiles closer to birds than to
       <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">
        crocodiles
       </a>
       (alternately
       <a href="/wiki/Avemetatarsalia" title="Avemetatarsalia">
        Avemetatarsalia
       </a>
       [=Panaves])
      </li>
      <li>
       Aves can mean the last common ancestor of all the currently living birds and all of its descendants (a "crown group"). (alternately
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neornithes" title="Neornithes">
        Neornithes
       </a>
       )
      </li>
     </ol>
     <p>
      Under the fourth definition
      <i>
       Archaeopteryx
      </i>
      is an avialan, and not a member of Aves. Gauthier's proposals have been adopted by many researchers in the field of paleontology and bird evolution, though the exact definitions applied have been inconsistent. Avialae, initially proposed to replace the traditional fossil content of Aves, is often used synonymously with the vernacular term "bird" by these researchers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nature-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most researchers define Avialae as branch-based clade, though definitions vary. Many authors have used a definition similar to "all
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theropod" title="Theropod">
       theropods
      </a>
      closer to birds than to
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Deinonychus" title="Deinonychus">
        Deinonychus
       </a>
      </i>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weishampel2004_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-weishampel2004-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-senter2007_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-senter2007-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Avialae is also occasionally defined as an
      <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_nomenclature#Phylogenetic_definitions" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature">
       apomorphy-based clade
      </a>
      (that is, one based on physical characteristics).
      <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Gauthier" title="Jacques Gauthier">
       Jacques Gauthier
      </a>
      , who named Avialae in 1986, re-defined it in 2001 as all dinosaurs that possessed feathered
      <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">
       wings
      </a>
      used in flapping
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_flight" title="Bird flight">
       flight
      </a>
      , and the birds that descended from them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gauthier.26dequeiroz2001_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gauthier.26dequeiroz2001-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gauthier1986_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gauthier1986-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dinosaurs_and_the_origin_of_birds">
       Dinosaurs and the origin of birds
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_birds" title="Origin of birds">
       Origin of birds
      </a>
     </div>
     <table style="border: 1px solid #ccc; background-color: white; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 0em; width: 248px; float: right; clear: right; vertical-align: middle">
      <tr>
       <td style="border: 1px solid #dedede; padding: 3px">
        <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="font-size:75%;line-height:80%">
         <tr>
          <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
           <span class="nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/Paraves" title="Paraves">
             Paraves
            </a>
           </span>
          </td>
          <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
           <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="">
            <tr>
             <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
              <br/>
             </td>
             <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
              <p>
               
               <a href="/wiki/Scansoriopterygidae" title="Scansoriopterygidae">
                Scansoriopterygidae
               </a>
              </p>
             </td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
             <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">
              <br/>
             </td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
             <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
              <br/>
             </td>
             <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
              <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="">
               <tr>
                <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                 <br/>
                </td>
                <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                 <p>
                  <span title="Extinct">
                   <span style="font-style:normal;">
                    <span style="font-weight:normal;">
                     
                    </span>
                   </span>
                  </span>
                  <i>
                   <a href="/wiki/Eosinopteryx" title="Eosinopteryx">
                    Eosinopteryx
                   </a>
                  </i>
                 </p>
                </td>
               </tr>
               <tr>
                <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                 <br/>
                </td>
               </tr>
               <tr>
                <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                 <span class="nowrap">
                  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eumaniraptora" title="Eumaniraptora">
                   Eumaniraptora
                  </a>
                 </span>
                </td>
                <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                 <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="">
                  <tr>
                   <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                    <br/>
                   </td>
                   <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                    <p>
                     <span title="Extinct">
                      <span style="font-style:normal;">
                       <span style="font-weight:normal;">
                        
                       </span>
                      </span>
                     </span>
                     <a href="/wiki/Dromaeosauridae" title="Dromaeosauridae">
                      Dromaeosauridae
                     </a>
                    </p>
                   </td>
                  </tr>
                  <tr>
                   <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                    <br/>
                   </td>
                  </tr>
                  <tr>
                   <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                    <br/>
                   </td>
                   <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                    <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="">
                     <tr>
                      <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                       <br/>
                      </td>
                      <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                       <p>
                        <span title="Extinct">
                         <span style="font-style:normal;">
                          <span style="font-weight:normal;">
                           
                          </span>
                         </span>
                        </span>
                        <a href="/wiki/Troodontidae" title="Troodontidae">
                         Troodontidae
                        </a>
                       </p>
                      </td>
                     </tr>
                     <tr>
                      <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                       <br/>
                      </td>
                     </tr>
                     <tr>
                      <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                       <br/>
                      </td>
                      <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                       <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="">
                        <tr>
                         <td style="width: 1.5em; border-bottom: 1px solid black; border-left: 0; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
                          <br/>
                         </td>
                         <td rowspan="2" style="border: 0; padding: 0;">
                          <p>
                           <a href="/wiki/Avialae" title="Avialae">
                            Avialae
                           </a>
                          </p>
                         </td>
                        </tr>
                        <tr>
                         <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                          <br/>
                         </td>
                        </tr>
                       </table>
                      </td>
                     </tr>
                     <tr>
                      <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                       <br/>
                      </td>
                     </tr>
                    </table>
                   </td>
                  </tr>
                  <tr>
                   <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                    <br/>
                   </td>
                  </tr>
                 </table>
                </td>
               </tr>
               <tr>
                <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                 <br/>
                </td>
               </tr>
              </table>
             </td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
             <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
              <br/>
             </td>
            </tr>
           </table>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
           <br/>
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; width: 248px; border: 0; padding: 0;">
        Cladogram following a phylogenetic study by Godefroit
        <i>
         et al.
        </i>
        , 2013.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_11-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Nature-11">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          11
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Based on fossil and biological evidence, most scientists accept that birds are a specialized subgroup of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theropod" title="Theropod">
       theropod
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">
       dinosaurs
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and more specifically, they are members of
      <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">
       Maniraptora
      </a>
      , a group of theropods which includes
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dromaeosaur" title="Dromaeosaur">
       dromaeosaurs
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oviraptorid" title="Oviraptorid">
       oviraptorids
      </a>
      , among others.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As scientists have discovered more theropods closely related to birds, the previously clear distinction between non-birds and birds has become blurred. Recent discoveries in the
      <a href="/wiki/Liaoning" title="Liaoning">
       Liaoning
      </a>
      Province of northeast China, which demonstrate many small theropod
      <a href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaur" title="Feathered dinosaur">
       feathered dinosaur
      </a>
      , contribute to this ambiguity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20140731_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AP-20140731-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140731_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140731-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The consensus view in contemporary
      <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">
       paleontology
      </a>
      is that the flying theropods, or
      <a href="/wiki/Avialae" title="Avialae">
       avialans
      </a>
      , are the closest relatives of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deinonychosaur" title="Deinonychosaur">
       deinonychosaurs
      </a>
      , which include dromaeosaurids and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troodontid" title="Troodontid">
       troodontids
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xiaotingia_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Xiaotingia-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Together, these form a group called
      <a href="/wiki/Paraves" title="Paraves">
       Paraves
      </a>
      . Some
      <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">
       basal
      </a>
      members of this group, such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Microraptor" title="Microraptor">
        Microraptor
       </a>
      </i>
      , have features which may have enabled them to glide or fly. The most basal deinonychosaurs were very small. This evidence raises the possibility that the ancestor of all paravians may have been
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arboreal" title="Arboreal">
       arboreal
      </a>
      , have been able to glide, or both.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AHTetal07_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AHTetal07-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xuetal2003_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-xuetal2003-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Unlike
      <i>
       Archaeopteryx
      </i>
      and the non-avialan feathered dinosaurs, who primarily ate meat, recent studies suggest that the first avialans were
      <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">
       omnivores
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confuciusornis_sanctus_(2).jpg">
        <img alt=" White slab of rock left with cracks and impression of bird feathers and bone, including long paired tail feathers" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Confuciusornis_sanctus_%282%29.jpg/220px-Confuciusornis_sanctus_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Confuciusornis_sanctus_%282%29.jpg/330px-Confuciusornis_sanctus_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Confuciusornis_sanctus_%282%29.jpg/440px-Confuciusornis_sanctus_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Confuciusornis_sanctus_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confuciusornis_sanctus" title="Confuciusornis sanctus">
          Confuciusornis sanctus
         </a>
        </i>
        , a Cretaceous bird from China
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Late_Jurassic" title="Late Jurassic">
       Late Jurassic
      </a>
      <i>
       Archaeopteryx
      </i>
      is well known as one of the first
      <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">
       transitional fossils
      </a>
      to be found, and it provided support for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_evolution" title="Theory of evolution">
       theory of evolution
      </a>
      in the late 19th century.
      <i>
       Archaeopteryx
      </i>
      was the first fossil to display both clearly traditional reptilian characteristics: teeth, clawed fingers, and a long, lizard-like tail, as well as wings with flight feathers similar to those of modern birds. It is not considered a direct ancestor of birds, though it is possibly closely related to the true ancestor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mayretal2007_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mayretal2007-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_evolution">
       Early evolution
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_fossil_bird_genera" title="List of fossil bird genera">
       List of fossil bird genera
      </a>

        Cladogram following the results of a phylogenetic study by Lefvre
        <i>
         et al.
        </i>
        , 2014.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curvipes2014_25-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-curvipes2014-25">

     <p>
      The earliest known avialan fossils come from the
      <a href="/wiki/Tiaojishan_Formation" title="Tiaojishan Formation">
       Tiaojishan Formation
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
       China
      </a>
      , which has been dated to the late
      <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">
       Jurassic
      </a>
      period (
      <a href="/wiki/Oxfordian_(stage)" title="Oxfordian (stage)">
       Oxfordian
      </a>
      stage), about 160 million years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature_11-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nature-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The avialan species from this time period include
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchiornis_huxleyi" title="Anchiornis huxleyi">
        Anchiornis huxleyi
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xiaotingia_zhengi" title="Xiaotingia zhengi">
        Xiaotingia zhengi
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurornis_xui" title="Aurornis xui">
        Aurornis xui
       </a>
      </i>
      . The well-known early avialan,
      <i>
       Archaeopteryx
      </i>
      , dates from slightly later Jurassic rocks (about 155 million years old) from
      <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
       Germany
      </a>
      . Many of these early avialans shared unusual anatomical features that may be ancestral to modern birds, but were later lost during bird evolution. These features include enlarged claws on the second toe which may have been held clear of the ground in life, and long feathers or "hind wings" covering the hind limbs and feet, which may have been used in aerial maneuvering.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhengetal2013_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-zhengetal2013-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Avialans diversified into a wide variety of forms during the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cretaceous_Period" title="Cretaceous Period">
       Cretaceous Period
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiappe2007_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-chiappe2007-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many groups retained
      <a href="/wiki/Symplesiomorphy" title="Symplesiomorphy">
       primitive characteristics
      </a>
      , such as clawed wings and teeth, though the latter were lost independently in a number of avialan groups, including modern birds (Aves). While the earliest forms, such as
      <i>
       Archaeopteryx
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Jeholornis" title="Jeholornis">
        Jeholornis
       </a>
      </i>
      , retained the long bony tails of their ancestors,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiappe2007_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-chiappe2007-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the tails of more advanced avialans were shortened with the advent of the
      <a href="/wiki/Pygostyle" title="Pygostyle">
       pygostyle
      </a>
      bone in the group
      <a href="/wiki/Pygostylia" title="Pygostylia">
       Pygostylia
      </a>
      . In the late Cretaceous, around 95 million years ago, the ancestor of all modern birds also evolved a better sense of smell.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

                                                </b>
                                               </p>

                            </td>

                          <br/>
                         </td>

                       <br/>
                      </td>
                     </tr>
                    </table>
                   </td>
                  </tr>
                  <tr>
                   <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                    <br/>
                   </td>
                  </tr>
                 </table>
                </td>
               </tr>
               <tr>
                <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
                 <br/>
                </td>
               </tr>
              </table>
             </td>
            </tr>
            <tr>
             <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
              <br/>
             </td>
            </tr>
           </table>
          </td>
         </tr>

          <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
           <br/>
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; width: 248px; border: 0; padding: 0;">
        Mesozoic bird phylogeny simplified after Wang
        <i>
         et al.
        </i>
        , 2015's phylogenetic analysis.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2015_29-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wang2015-29">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          29
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The first large, diverse lineage of short-tailed avialans to evolve were the
      <a href="/wiki/Enantiornithes" title="Enantiornithes">
       enantiornithes
      </a>
      , or "opposite birds", so named because the construction of their shoulder bones was in reverse to that of modern birds. Enantiornithes occupied a wide array of ecological niches, from sand-probing shorebirds and fish-eaters to tree-dwelling forms and seed-eaters. While they were the dominant group of avialans during the Cretaceous period, enantiornithes became extinct along with many other dinosaur groups at the end of the
      <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">
       Mesozoic
      </a>
      era.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiappe2007_27-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-chiappe2007-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many species of the second major avialan lineage to diversify, the
      <a href="/wiki/Euornithes" title="Euornithes">
       Euornithes
      </a>
      (meaning "true birds", because they include the ancestors of modern birds), were semi-aquatic and specialized in eating fish and other small aquatic organisms. Unlike the enantiornithes, which dominated land-based and arboreal habitats, most early euornithes lacked perching adaptations and seem to have included shorebird-like species, waders, and swimming and diving species. The later included the superficially
      <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">
       gull
      </a>
      -like
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ichthyornis" title="Ichthyornis">
        Ichthyornis
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hesperornithiformes" title="Hesperornithiformes">
       Hesperornithiformes
      </a>
      , which became so well adapted to hunting fish in marine environments that they lost the ability to fly and became primarily aquatic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiappe2007_27-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-chiappe2007-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The early euornithes also saw the development of many traits associated with modern birds, like strongly keeled breastbones, toothless, beaked portions of their jaws (though most non-avian euornithes retained teeth in other parts of the jaws).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-louchart2011_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-louchart2011-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Euornithes also included the first avialans to develop true
      <a href="/wiki/Pygostyle" title="Pygostyle">
       pygostyle
      </a>
      and a fully mobile fan of tail feathers,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yixianornis_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-yixianornis-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which may have replaced the "hind wing" as the primary mode of aerial maneuverability and braking in flight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhengetal2013_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-zhengetal2013-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diversification_of_modern_birds">
       Diversification of modern birds
      </span>
     </h3>

       Sibley-Ahlquist taxonomy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_classification" title="Dinosaur classification">
       dinosaur classification
      </a>
     </div>

         <tr>
          <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
           <br/>
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; width: 248px; border: 0; padding: 0;">
        Basal divergences of modern birds
        <br/>
        based on
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sibley-Ahlquist_taxonomy" title="Sibley-Ahlquist taxonomy">
         Sibley-Ahlquist taxonomy
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      All modern birds lie within the
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_group" title="Crown group">
       crown group
      </a>
      Aves (alternately Neornithes), which has two subdivisions: the
      <a href="/wiki/Palaeognathae" title="Palaeognathae">
       Palaeognathae
      </a>
      , which includes the flightless
      <a href="/wiki/Ratite" title="Ratite">
       ratites
      </a>
      (such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">
       ostriches
      </a>
      ) and the weak-flying
      <a href="/wiki/Tinamou" title="Tinamou">
       tinamous
      </a>
      , and the extremely diverse
      <a href="/wiki/Neognathae" title="Neognathae">
       Neognathae
      </a>
      , containing all other birds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theropoda_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Theropoda-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These two subdivisions are often given the
      <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">
       rank
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superorder" title="Superorder">
       superorder
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although
      <a href="/wiki/Bradley_C._Livezey" title="Bradley C. Livezey">
       Livezey
      </a>
      and Zusi assigned them "cohort" rank.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theropoda_7-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Theropoda-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Depending on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_taxonomy" title="Alpha taxonomy">
       taxonomic
      </a>
      viewpoint, the number of known living bird species varies anywhere from 9,800
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to 10,050.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Due largely to the discovery of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Vegavis" title="Vegavis">
        Vegavis
       </a>
      </i>
      , a late Cretaceous neognath member of the
      <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">
       duck
      </a>
      lineage, Aves is known to have split into several modern lineages by the end of the Mesozoic era.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies using a "morphological clock" analysis have estimated that the actual origin of modern birds probably occurred slightly earlier than the earliest known fossils, during the mid-Cretaceous period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divergence_1-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-divergence-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The earliest divergence within the Neognathes was that of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galloanserae" title="Galloanserae">
       Galloanserae
      </a>
      , the superorder containing the
      <a href="/wiki/Anseriformes" title="Anseriformes">
       Anseriformes
      </a>
      (ducks,
      <a href="/wiki/Goose" title="Goose">
       geese
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">
       swans
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Screamer" title="Screamer">
       screamers
      </a>
      ) and the
      <a href="/wiki/Galliformes" title="Galliformes">
       Galliformes
      </a>
      (the
      <a href="/wiki/Pheasant" title="Pheasant">
       pheasants
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">
       grouse
      </a>
      , and their allies, together with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mound_builder_(bird)" title="Mound builder (bird)">
       mound builders
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Guan_(bird)" title="Guan (bird)">
       guans
      </a>
      and their allies). The earliest fossil remains of true birds come from the possible galliform
      <i>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Austinornis_lentus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Austinornis lentus (page does not exist)">
        Austinornis lentus
       </a>
      </i>
      , dated to about 85 million years ago,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but the dates for the actual splits are much debated by scientists. The Aves are agreed to have evolved in the Cretaceous, and the split between the Galloanseri from other Neognathes occurred before the
      <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="CretaceousPaleogene extinction event">
       CretaceousPaleogene extinction event
      </a>
      , but there are different opinions about whether the
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_radiation" title="Evolutionary radiation">
       radiation
      </a>
      of the remaining Neognathes occurred before or after the extinction of the other dinosaurs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ericson_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ericson-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This disagreement is in part caused by a divergence in the evidence; molecular dating suggests a Cretaceous radiation, while
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">
       fossil
      </a>
      evidence supports a
      <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">
       Cenozoic
      </a>
      radiation. Attempts to reconcile the molecular and fossil evidence have proved controversial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ericson_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ericson-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_bird_orders">
       Classification of bird orders
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_birds" title="List of birds">
       List of birds
      </a>
     </div>

      <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">

      </a>
      of modern bird relationships based on Jarvis, E.D.
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (2014)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarvis2014_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jarvis2014-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with some
      <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">
       clade
      </a>
      names after Yury, T.
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (2013).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>

        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

                                 <tr>
                                  <td style="border-left: 1px solid black; border-top: 0; border-right: 0; border-bottom: 1px solid black; padding: 0 0.2em; vertical-align: bottom; text-align: center;">
