      <tr>
       <td style="border: 0; padding: 0; vertical-align: top;">
        <br/>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The classification of birds is a contentious issue.
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sibley" title="Charles Sibley">
       Sibley
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jon_Ahlquist" title="Jon Ahlquist">
       Ahlquist
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       Phylogeny and Classification of Birds
      </i>
      (1990) is a landmark work on the classification of birds,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although it is frequently debated and constantly revised. Most evidence seems to suggest the assignment of orders is accurate,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but scientists disagree about the relationships between the orders themselves; evidence from modern bird anatomy, fossils and DNA have all been brought to bear on the problem, but no strong consensus has emerged. More recently, new fossil and molecular evidence is providing an increasingly clear picture of the evolution of modern bird orders. The most recent effort is drawn above and is based on
      <a href="/wiki/Whole_genome_sequencing" title="Whole genome sequencing">
       whole genome sequencing
      </a>
      of 48 representative species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarvis2014_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jarvis2014-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">
       Distribution
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_birds_by_region" title="Lists of birds by region">
       Lists of birds by region
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lists_of_birds_by_population" title="Lists of birds by population">
       Lists of birds by population
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow04.jpg">
        <img alt=" small bird withpale belly and breast and patterned wing and head stands on concrete" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/House_sparrow04.jpg/220px-House_sparrow04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/House_sparrow04.jpg/330px-House_sparrow04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/House_sparrow04.jpg/440px-House_sparrow04.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:House_sparrow04.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The range of the
        <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">
         house sparrow
        </a>
        has expanded dramatically due to human activities.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
         <a href="#cite_note-44">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          44
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Birds live and breed in most terrestrial habitats and on all seven continents, reaching their southern extreme in the
      <a href="/wiki/Snow_petrel" title="Snow petrel">
       snow petrel's
      </a>
      breeding colonies up to 440 kilometres (270mi) inland in
      <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">
       Antarctica
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The highest bird
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
       diversity
      </a>
      occurs in tropical regions. It was earlier thought that this high diversity was the result of higher
      <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">
       speciation
      </a>
      rates in the tropics, however recent studies found higher speciation rates in the high latitudes that were offset by greater
      <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">
       extinction
      </a>
      rates than in the tropics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several families of birds have adapted to life both on the world's oceans and in them, with some
      <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">
       seabird
      </a>
      species coming ashore only to breed
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burger_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Burger-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and some
      <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">
       penguins
      </a>
      have been recorded diving up to 300 metres (980ft).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many bird species have established breeding populations in areas to which they have been
      <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
       introduced
      </a>
      by humans. Some of these introductions have been deliberate; the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ring-necked_pheasant" title="Ring-necked pheasant">
       ring-necked pheasant
      </a>
      , for example, has been introduced around the world as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_bird" title="Game bird">
       game bird
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Others have been accidental, such as the establishment of wild
      <a href="/wiki/Monk_parakeet" title="Monk parakeet">
       monk parakeets
      </a>
      in several North American cities after their escape from captivity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some species, including
      <a href="/wiki/Cattle_egret" title="Cattle egret">
       cattle egret
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Yellow-headed_caracara" title="Yellow-headed caracara">
       yellow-headed caracara
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Galah" title="Galah">
       galah
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      have
      <a href="/wiki/Avian_range_expansion" title="Avian range expansion">
       spread naturally
      </a>
      far beyond their original ranges as
      <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">
       agricultural practices
      </a>
      created suitable new habitat.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">
       Anatomy and physiology
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy" title="Bird anatomy">
       Bird anatomy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_vision" title="Bird vision">
       Bird vision
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birdmorphology.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="600" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Birdmorphology.svg/300px-Birdmorphology.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Birdmorphology.svg/450px-Birdmorphology.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Birdmorphology.svg/600px-Birdmorphology.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birdmorphology.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        External anatomy of a bird (example:
        <a href="/wiki/Yellow-wattled_lapwing" title="Yellow-wattled lapwing">
         yellow-wattled lapwing
        </a>
        ): 1 Beak, 2 Head, 3 Iris, 4 Pupil, 5 Mantle, 6 Lesser
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covert_(feather)" title="Covert (feather)">
         coverts
        </a>
        , 7 Scapulars, 8 Median coverts, 9 Tertials, 10 Rump, 11 Primaries, 12 Vent, 13 Thigh, 14 Tibio-tarsal articulation, 15 Tarsus, 16 Foot, 17 Tibia, 18 Belly, 19 Flanks, 20 Breast, 21 Throat, 22 Wattle, 23 Eyestripe
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Compared with other vertebrates, birds have a
      <a href="/wiki/Body_plan" title="Body plan">
       body plan
      </a>
      that shows many unusual adaptations, mostly to facilitate
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_flight" title="Bird flight">
       flight
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Skeletal_system">
       Skeletal system
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The skeleton consists of very lightweight bones. They have large air-filled cavities (called pneumatic cavities) which connect with the
      <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">
       respiratory system
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The skull bones in adults are fused and do not show
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cranial_sutures" title="Cranial sutures">
       cranial sutures
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Orbit_(anatomy)" title="Orbit (anatomy)">
       orbits
      </a>
      are large and separated by a bony
      <a href="/wiki/Septum" title="Septum">
       septum
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">
       spine
      </a>
      has cervical, thoracic, lumbar and caudal regions with the number of cervical (neck) vertebrae highly variable and especially flexible, but movement is reduced in the anterior
      <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_vertebrae" title="Thoracic vertebrae">
       thoracic vertebrae
      </a>
      and absent in the later vertebrae.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The last few are fused with the
      <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">
       pelvis
      </a>
      to form the
      <a href="/wiki/Synsacrum" title="Synsacrum">
       synsacrum
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The ribs are flattened and the
      <a href="/wiki/Sternum" title="Sternum">
       sternum
      </a>
      is keeled for the attachment of flight muscles except in the flightless bird orders. The forelimbs are modified into wings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Excretory_system">
       Excretory system
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Like the
      <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">
       reptiles
      </a>
      , birds are primarily uricotelic, that is, their
      <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">
       kidneys
      </a>
      extract nitrogenous wastes from their bloodstream and excrete it as
      <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">
       uric acid
      </a>
      instead of
      <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">
       urea
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
       ammonia
      </a>
      via the ureters into the intestine. Birds do not have a
      <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">
       urinary bladder
      </a>
      or external urethral opening and (with exception of the
      <a href="/wiki/Ostrich#Description" title="Ostrich">
       ostrich
      </a>
      ) uric acid is excreted along with feces as a semisolid waste.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coprodeum_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-coprodeum-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, birds such as hummingbirds can be facultatively ammonotelic, excreting most of the nitrogenous wastes as ammonia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They also excrete
      <a href="/wiki/Creatine" title="Creatine">
       creatine
      </a>
      , rather than
      <a href="/wiki/Creatinine" title="Creatinine">
       creatinine
      </a>
      like mammals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This material, as well as the output of the intestines, emerges from the bird's
      <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">
       cloaca
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The cloaca is a multi-purpose opening: waste is expelled through it, most birds mate by
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy#Reproduction" title="Bird anatomy">
       joining cloaca
      </a>
      , and females lay eggs from it. In addition, many species of birds regurgitate
      <a href="/wiki/Pellet_(ornithology)" title="Pellet (ornithology)">
       pellets
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Males within
      <a href="/wiki/Palaeognathae" title="Palaeognathae">
       Palaeognathae
      </a>
      (with the exception of the
      <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">
       kiwis
      </a>
      ), the
      <a href="/wiki/Anseriformes" title="Anseriformes">
       Anseriformes
      </a>
      (with the exception of
      <a href="/wiki/Screamer" title="Screamer">
       screamers
      </a>
      ), and in rudimentary forms in
      <a href="/wiki/Galliformes" title="Galliformes">
       Galliformes
      </a>
      (but fully developed in
      <a href="/wiki/Cracidae" title="Cracidae">
       Cracidae
      </a>
      ) possess a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bird_penis" title="Bird penis">
       penis
      </a>
      , which is never present in
      <a href="/wiki/Neoaves" title="Neoaves">
       Neoaves
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The length is thought to be related to
      <a href="/wiki/Sperm_competition" title="Sperm competition">
       sperm competition
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When not copulating, it is hidden within the
      <a href="/wiki/Proctodeum" title="Proctodeum">
       proctodeum
      </a>
      compartment within the cloaca, just inside the vent. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">
       digestive system
      </a>
      of birds is unique, with a
      <a href="/wiki/Crop_(anatomy)" title="Crop (anatomy)">
       crop
      </a>
      for storage and a
      <a href="/wiki/Gizzard" title="Gizzard">
       gizzard
      </a>
      that contains swallowed stones for grinding food to compensate for the lack of teeth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most birds are highly adapted for rapid digestion to aid with flight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Attenborough_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Attenborough-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some migratory birds have adapted to use protein from many parts of their bodies, including protein from the intestines, as additional energy during migration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battley_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Battley-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_and_circulatory_systems">
       Respiratory and circulatory systems
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Birds have one of the most complex
      <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">
       respiratory systems
      </a>
      of all animal groups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Upon inhalation, 75% of the fresh air bypasses the lungs and flows directly into a posterior
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parabronchi" title="Parabronchi">
       air sac
      </a>
      which extends from the lungs and connects with air spaces in the bones and fills them with air. The other 25% of the air goes directly into the lungs. When the bird exhales, the used air flows out of the lung and the stored fresh air from the posterior air sac is simultaneously forced into the lungs. Thus, a bird's lungs receive a constant supply of fresh air during both inhalation and exhalation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sound production is achieved using the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrinx_(biology)" title="Syrinx (biology)">
       syrinx
      </a>
      , a muscular chamber incorporating multiple tympanic membranes which diverges from the lower end of the trachea;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suthers_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Suthers-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the trachea being elongated in some species, increasing the volume of vocalizations and the perception of the bird's size.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitch_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fitch-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In birds, the main arteries taking blood away from the heart originate from the right
      <a href="/wiki/Aortic_arches" title="Aortic arches">
       aortic arch
      </a>
      (or pharyngeal arch), unlike in the mammals where the left aortic arch forms this part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">
       aorta
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The postcava receives blood from the limbs via the renal portal system. Unlike in mammals, the circulating
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_blood_cells" title="Red blood cells">
       red blood cells
      </a>
      in birds retain their
      <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">
       nucleus
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_type_and_features">
       Heart type and features
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The avian circulatory system is driven by a four-chambered, myogenic heart contained in a fibrous pericardial sac. This pericardial sac is filled with a
      <a href="/wiki/Serous_fluid" title="Serous fluid">
       serous fluid
      </a>
      for lubrication.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittow_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Whittow-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The heart itself is divided into a right and left half, each with an
      <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">
       atrium
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">
       ventricle
      </a>
      . The atrium and ventricles of each side are separated by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atrioventricular_valves" title="Atrioventricular valves">
       atrioventricular valves
      </a>
      which prevent back flow from one chamber to the next during contraction. Being myogenic, the heart's pace is maintained by pacemaker cells found in the sinoatrial node, located on the right atrium. The
      <a href="/wiki/Sinoatrial_node" title="Sinoatrial node">
       sinoatrial node
      </a>
      uses calcium to cause a depolarizing signal transduction pathway from the atrium through right and left atrioventricular bundle which communicates contraction to the ventricles. The avian heart also consists of muscular arches that are made up of thick bundles of muscular layers. Much like a mammalian heart, the avian heart is composed of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocardial" title="Endocardial">
       endocardial
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myocardial" title="Myocardial">
       myocardial
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicardial" title="Epicardial">
       epicardial
      </a>
      layers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittow_75-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Whittow-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The atrium walls tend to be thinner than the ventricle walls, due to the intense ventricular contraction used to pump oxygenated blood throughout the body. Avian hearts are generally larger than mammalian hearts when compared to body mass. This adaptation allows more blood to be pumped to meet the high metabolic need associated with flight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoagstrom_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoagstrom-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">
       Organization
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Birds have a very efficient system for diffusing oxygen into the blood; birds have a ten times greater surface area to gas exchange volume than mammals. As a result, birds have more blood in their capillaries per unit of volume of lung than a mammal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoagstrom_76-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hoagstrom-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The arteries are composed of thick elastic muscles to withstand the pressure of the ventricular constriction, and become more rigid as they move away from the heart. Blood moves through the arteries, which undergo
      <a href="/wiki/Vasoconstriction" title="Vasoconstriction">
       vasoconstriction
      </a>
      , and into arterioles which act as a transportation system to distribute primarily oxygen as well as nutrients to all tissues of the body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hill-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the arterioles move away from the heart and into individual organs and tissues they are further divided to increase surface area and slow blood flow. Travelling through the arterioles blood moves into the capillaries where gas exchange can occur. Capillaries are organized into capillary beds in tissues, it is here that blood exchanges oxygen for carbon dioxide waste. In the capillary beds blood flow is slowed to allow maximum diffusion of oxygen into the tissues. Once the blood has become deoxygenated it travels through venules then veins and back to the heart. Veins, unlike arteries, are thin and rigid as they do not need to withstand extreme pressure. As blood travels through the venules to the veins a funneling occurs called
      <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">
       vasodilation
      </a>
      bringing blood back to the heart.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_77-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hill-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Once the blood reaches the heart it moves first into the right atrium, then the right ventricle to be pumped through the lungs for further gas exchange of carbon dioxide waste for oxygen. Oxygenated blood then flows from the lungs through the left atrium to the left ventricle where it is pumped out to the body.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bird_blink-edit.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="1380" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Bird_blink-edit.jpg/300px-Bird_blink-edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Bird_blink-edit.jpg/450px-Bird_blink-edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Bird_blink-edit.jpg/600px-Bird_blink-edit.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bird_blink-edit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Nictitating_membrane" title="Nictitating membrane">
         nictitating membrane
        </a>
        as it covers the eye of a
        <a href="/wiki/Masked_lapwing" title="Masked lapwing">
         masked lapwing
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_system">
       Nervous system
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">
       nervous system
      </a>
      is large relative to the bird's size.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most developed part of the brain is the one that controls the flight-related functions, while the
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebellum" title="Cerebellum">
       cerebellum
      </a>
      coordinates movement and the
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum">
       cerebrum
      </a>
      controls behaviour patterns, navigation, mating and
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">
       nest
      </a>
      building. Most birds have a poor
      <a href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">
       sense of smell
      </a>
      with notable exceptions including
      <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">
       kiwis
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/New_World_vulture" title="New World vulture">
       New World vultures
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avian_Sense_of_Smell_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avian_Sense_of_Smell-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tubenoses" title="Tubenoses">
       tubenoses
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The avian
      <a href="/wiki/Visual_system" title="Visual system">
       visual system
      </a>
      is usually highly developed. Water birds have special flexible lenses, allowing accommodation for vision in air and water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some species also have dual
      <a href="/wiki/Fovea_centralis" title="Fovea centralis">
       fovea
      </a>
      . Birds are
      <a href="/wiki/Tetrachromacy" title="Tetrachromacy">
       tetrachromatic
      </a>
      , possessing
      <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
       ultraviolet
      </a>
      (UV) sensitive
      <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">
       cone cells
      </a>
      in the eye as well as green, red and blue ones.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This allows them to perceive ultraviolet light, which is involved in courtship. Birds have
      <a class="external text" href="https://birdsnews.com/2014/scientists-document-light-sensitive-eye-within-brain-birds/#.U8ArB_ldWCl" rel="nofollow">
       specialized light-sensing cells deep in their brains
      </a>
      that respond to light without input from eyes or other sensory neurons. These photo-receptive cells in the hypothalamus are involved in detecting the longer days of spring, and thus regulate breeding activities.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many birds show plumage patterns in ultraviolet that are invisible to the human eye; some birds whose sexes appear similar to the naked eye are distinguished by the presence of
      <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
       ultraviolet
      </a>
      reflective patches on their feathers. Male
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_tit" title="Blue tit">
       blue tits
      </a>
      have an ultraviolet reflective crown patch which is displayed in courtship by posturing and raising of their nape feathers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ultraviolet light is also used in foraging
      <a href="/wiki/Kestrel" title="Kestrel">
       kestrels
      </a>
      have been shown to search for prey by detecting the UV reflective urine trail marks left on the ground by rodents.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The eyelids of a bird are not used in blinking. Instead the eye is lubricated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Nictitating_membrane" title="Nictitating membrane">
       nictitating membrane
      </a>
      , a third eyelid that moves horizontally.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The nictitating membrane also covers the eye and acts as a
      <a href="/wiki/Contact_lens" title="Contact lens">
       contact lens
      </a>
      in many aquatic birds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The bird
      <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">
       retina
      </a>
      has a fan shaped blood supply system called the
      <a href="/wiki/Pecten_oculi" title="Pecten oculi">
       pecten
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most birds cannot move their eyes, although there are exceptions, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_cormorant" title="Great cormorant">
       great cormorant
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Birds with eyes on the sides of their heads have a wide
      <a href="/wiki/Visual_field" title="Visual field">
       visual field
      </a>
      , while birds with eyes on the front of their heads, such as owls, have
      <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">
       binocular vision
      </a>
      and can estimate the
      <a href="/wiki/Depth_of_field" title="Depth of field">
       depth of field
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The avian
      <a href="/wiki/Ear" title="Ear">
       ear
      </a>
      lacks external
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinna_(anatomy)" title="Pinna (anatomy)">
       pinnae
      </a>
      but is covered by feathers, although in some birds, such as the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Asio" title="Asio">
        Asio
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">
        Bubo
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Scops_owl" title="Scops owl">
        Otus
       </a>
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">
       owls
      </a>
      , these feathers form tufts which resemble ears. The
      <a href="/wiki/Inner_ear" title="Inner ear">
       inner ear
      </a>
      has a
      <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">
       cochlea
      </a>
      , but it is not spiral as in mammals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Defence">
       Defence
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A few species are able to use chemical defenses against predators; some
      <a href="/wiki/Procellariiformes" title="Procellariiformes">
       Procellariiformes
      </a>
      can eject an unpleasant
      <a href="/wiki/Stomach_oil" title="Stomach oil">
       oil
      </a>
      against an aggressor,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and some species of
      <a href="/wiki/Pitohui" title="Pitohui">
       pitohuis
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">
       New Guinea
      </a>
      have a powerful
      <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxin" title="Neurotoxin">
       neurotoxin
      </a>
      in their skin and feathers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chromosomes">
       Chromosomes
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Birds have two sexes: male and female. The sex of birds is determined by the
      <a href="/wiki/ZW_sex-determination_system" title="ZW sex-determination system">
       Z and W sex chromosomes
      </a>
      , rather than by the X and Y chromosomes present in mammals. Male birds have two Z chromosomes (ZZ), and female birds have a W chromosome and a Z chromosome (WZ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In nearly all species of birds, an individual's sex is determined at fertilization. However, one recent study demonstrated
      <a href="/wiki/Temperature-dependent_sex_determination" title="Temperature-dependent sex determination">
       temperature-dependent sex determination
      </a>
      among the
      <a href="/wiki/Australian_brushturkey" title="Australian brushturkey">
       Australian brushturkey
      </a>
      , for which higher temperatures during incubation resulted in a higher female-to-male
      <a href="/wiki/Sex_ratio" title="Sex ratio">
       sex ratio
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This, however, was later proven to not be the case. These birds do not exhibit temperature-dependent sex determination, but temperature-dependent sex mortality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Feathers.2C_plumage.2C_and_scales">
       Feathers, plumage, and scales
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">
       Feather
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">
       Flight feather
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:African_Scops_owl.jpg">
        <img alt="Owl with eyes closed in front of similarly coloured tree trunk partly obscured by green leaves" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/African_Scops_owl.jpg/220px-African_Scops_owl.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/African_Scops_owl.jpg/330px-African_Scops_owl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/African_Scops_owl.jpg/440px-African_Scops_owl.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:African_Scops_owl.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The plumage of the
        <a href="/wiki/African_scops_owl" title="African scops owl">
         African scops owl
        </a>
        allows it to blend in with its surroundings.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Feathers are a feature characteristic of birds (though also present in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feathered_dinosaurs" title="Feathered dinosaurs">
       some dinosaurs
      </a>
      not currently considered to be true birds). They facilitate
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_flight" title="Bird flight">
       flight
      </a>
      , provide insulation that aids in
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">
       thermoregulation
      </a>
      , and are used in display, camouflage, and signaling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are several types of feathers, each serving its own set of purposes. Feathers are epidermal growths attached to the skin and arise only in specific tracts of skin called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pterylae" title="Pterylae">
       pterylae
      </a>
      . The distribution pattern of these feather tracts (pterylosis) is used in taxonomy and systematics. The arrangement and appearance of feathers on the body, called
      <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">
       plumage
      </a>
      , may vary within species by age,
      <a href="/wiki/Social_status" title="Social status">
       social status
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">
       sex
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Plumage is regularly
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moult" title="Moult">
       moulted
      </a>
      ; the standard plumage of a bird that has moulted after breeding is known as the "non-breeding" plumage, orin the
      <a href="/wiki/Humphrey-Parkes_terminology" title="Humphrey-Parkes terminology">
       Humphrey-Parkes terminology
      </a>
      "basic" plumage; breeding plumages or variations of the basic plumage are known under the Humphrey-Parkes system as "alternate" plumages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Moulting is annual in most species, although some may have two moults a year, and large birds of prey may moult only once every few years. Moulting patterns vary across species. In passerines,
      <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">
       flight feathers
      </a>
      are replaced one at a time with the innermost primary being the first. When the fifth of sixth primary is replaced, the outermost tertiaries begin to drop. After the innermost tertiaries are moulted, the secondaries starting from the innermost begin to drop and this proceeds to the outer feathers (centrifugal moult). The greater primary coverts are moulted in synchrony with the primary that they overlap.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pettingill_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pettingill-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A small number of species, such as ducks and geese, lose all of their flight feathers at once, temporarily becoming flightless.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-debeeretal_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-debeeretal-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As a general rule, the tail feathers are moulted and replaced starting with the innermost pair.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pettingill_95-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pettingill-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Centripetal moults of tail feathers are however seen in the
      <a href="/wiki/Phasianidae" title="Phasianidae">
       Phasianidae
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The centrifugal moult is modified in the tail feathers of
      <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">
       woodpeckers
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Treecreeper" title="Treecreeper">
       treecreepers
      </a>
      , in that it begins with the second innermost pair of feathers and finishes with the central pair of feathers so that the bird maintains a functional climbing tail.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pettingill_95-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-pettingill-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The general pattern seen in
      <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">
       passerines
      </a>
      is that the primaries are replaced outward, secondaries inward, and the tail from center outward.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before nesting, the females of most bird species gain a bare
      <a href="/wiki/Brood_patch" title="Brood patch">
       brood patch
      </a>
      by losing feathers close to the belly. The skin there is well supplied with blood vessels and helps the bird in incubation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_Lory_(Eos_bornea)-6.jpg">
        <img alt="Red parrot with yellow bill and wing feathers in bill" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="683" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Red_Lory_%28Eos_bornea%29-6.jpg/170px-Red_Lory_%28Eos_bornea%29-6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Red_Lory_%28Eos_bornea%29-6.jpg/255px-Red_Lory_%28Eos_bornea%29-6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Red_Lory_%28Eos_bornea%29-6.jpg/340px-Red_Lory_%28Eos_bornea%29-6.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_Lory_(Eos_bornea)-6.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Red_lory" title="Red lory">
         Red lory
        </a>
        preening
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Feathers require maintenance and birds preen or groom them daily, spending an average of around 9% of their daily time on this.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The bill is used to brush away foreign particles and to apply
      <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">
       waxy
      </a>
      secretions from the
      <a href="/wiki/Uropygial_gland" title="Uropygial gland">
       uropygial gland
      </a>
      ; these secretions protect the feathers' flexibility and act as an
      <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">
       antimicrobial agent
      </a>
      , inhibiting the growth of feather-degrading
      <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">
       bacteria
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This may be supplemented with the secretions of
      <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">
       formic acid
      </a>
      from ants, which birds receive through a behaviour known as
      <a href="/wiki/Anting_(bird_activity)" title="Anting (bird activity)">
       anting
      </a>
      , to remove feather parasites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_anatomy#Scales" title="Bird anatomy">
       scales
      </a>
      of birds are composed of the same keratin as beaks, claws, and spurs. They are found mainly on the toes and
      <a href="/wiki/Metatarsus" title="Metatarsus">
       metatarsus
      </a>
      , but may be found further up on the ankle in some birds. Most bird scales do not overlap significantly, except in the cases of
      <a href="/wiki/Kingfisher" title="Kingfisher">
       kingfishers
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">
       woodpeckers
      </a>
      . The scales of birds are thought to be
      <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">
       homologous
      </a>
      to those of reptiles and mammals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Flight">
       Flight
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_flight" title="Bird flight">
       Bird flight
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Restless_flycatcher04.jpg">
        <img alt="Black bird with white chest in flight with wings facing down and tail fanned and down pointing" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Restless_flycatcher04.jpg/220px-Restless_flycatcher04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Restless_flycatcher04.jpg/330px-Restless_flycatcher04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Restless_flycatcher04.jpg/440px-Restless_flycatcher04.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Restless_flycatcher04.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Restless_flycatcher" title="Restless flycatcher">
         Restless flycatcher
        </a>
        in the downstroke of flapping flight
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Most birds can
      <a href="/wiki/Flying_and_gliding_animals" title="Flying and gliding animals">
       fly
      </a>
      , which distinguishes them from almost all other vertebrate classes. Flight is the primary means of locomotion for most bird species and is used for breeding, feeding, and predator avoidance and escape. Birds have various adaptations for flight, including a lightweight skeleton, two large flight muscles, the pectoralis (which accounts for 15% of the total mass of the bird) and the supracoracoideus, as well as a modified forelimb (
      <a href="/wiki/Wing" title="Wing">
       wing
      </a>
      ) that serves as an
      <a href="/wiki/Airfoil" title="Airfoil">
       aerofoil
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wing shape and size generally determine a bird species' type of flight; many birds combine powered, flapping flight with less energy-intensive soaring flight. About 60 extant bird species are
      <a href="/wiki/Flightless_bird" title="Flightless bird">
       flightless
      </a>
      , as were many extinct birds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Flightlessness often arises in birds on isolated islands, probably due to limited resources and the absence of land predators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though flightless, penguins use similar musculature and movements to "fly" through the water, as do
      <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">
       auks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Shearwater" title="Shearwater">
       shearwaters
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Dipper" title="Dipper">
       dippers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Weight_and_longevity">
       Weight and longevity
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The diversity of characteristics of birds is great, sometimes even in closely related species. The weight and longevity of several species are compared in the table below.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnAge_108-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AnAge-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_109-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ADW-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:center">
      <tr>
       <th>
        Species
       </th>
       <th>
        Adult weight
        <p>
         (grams)
        </p>
       </th>

       </th>
      </tr>
