     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">
       Behaviour
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Most birds are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diurnal_animal" title="Diurnal animal">
       diurnal
      </a>
      , but some birds, such as many species of
      <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">
       owls
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nightjar" title="Nightjar">
       nightjars
      </a>
      , are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nocturnal" title="Nocturnal">
       nocturnal
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular" title="Crepuscular">
       crepuscular
      </a>
      (active during twilight hours), and many coastal
      <a href="/wiki/Wader" title="Wader">
       waders
      </a>
      feed when the tides are appropriate, by day or night.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diet_and_feeding">
       Diet and feeding
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BirdBeaksA.svg">
        <img alt=" Illustration of the heads of 16 types of birds with different shapes and sizes of beak" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="465" height="512" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/BirdBeaksA.svg/170px-BirdBeaksA.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/BirdBeaksA.svg/255px-BirdBeaksA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/BirdBeaksA.svg/340px-BirdBeaksA.svg.png 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BirdBeaksA.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Feeding adaptations in beaks
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Birds' diets are varied and often include
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nectar_(plant)" title="Nectar (plant)">
       nectar
      </a>
      , fruit, plants, seeds,
      <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">
       carrion
      </a>
      , and various small animals, including other birds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because birds have no teeth, their
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">
       digestive system
      </a>
      is adapted to process
      <a href="/wiki/Mastication" title="Mastication">
       unmasticated
      </a>
      food items that are swallowed whole.
     </p>
     <p>
      Birds that employ many strategies to obtain food or feed on a variety of food items are called generalists, while others that concentrate time and effort on specific food items or have a single strategy to obtain food are considered specialists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Birds' feeding strategies vary by species. Many birds
      <a href="/wiki/Gleaning_(birds)" title="Gleaning (birds)">
       glean
      </a>
      for insects, invertebrates, fruit, or seeds. Some hunt insects by suddenly attacking from a branch. Those species that seek
      <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">
       pest
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">
       insects
      </a>
      are considered beneficial 'biological control agents' and their presence encouraged in
      <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">
       biological pest control
      </a>
      programs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lwa001_111-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lwa001-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nectar feeders such as
      <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">
       hummingbirds
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sunbird" title="Sunbird">
       sunbirds
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lories_and_lorikeets" title="Lories and lorikeets">
       lories, and lorikeets
      </a>
      amongst others have specially adapted brushy tongues and in many cases bills designed to fit
      <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">
       co-adapted
      </a>
      flowers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">
       Kiwis
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shorebird" title="Shorebird">
       shorebirds
      </a>
      with long bills probe for invertebrates; shorebirds' varied bill lengths and feeding methods result in the separation of
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">
       ecological niches
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Loon" title="Loon">
       Loons
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Diving_duck" title="Diving duck">
       diving ducks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">
       penguins
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auks" title="Auks">
       auks
      </a>
      pursue their prey underwater, using their wings or feet for propulsion,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burger_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Burger-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while aerial predators such as
      <a href="/wiki/Sulidae" title="Sulidae">
       sulids
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kingfisher" title="Kingfisher">
       kingfishers
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tern" title="Tern">
       terns
      </a>
      plunge dive after their prey.
      <a href="/wiki/Flamingo" title="Flamingo">
       Flamingos
      </a>
      , three species of
      <a href="/wiki/Prion_(bird)" title="Prion (bird)">
       prion
      </a>
      , and some ducks are
      <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">
       filter feeders
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geese" title="Geese">
       Geese
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dabbling_duck" title="Dabbling duck">
       dabbling ducks
      </a>
      are primarily grazers.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some species, including
      <a href="/wiki/Frigatebird" title="Frigatebird">
       frigatebirds
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">
       gulls
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Skua" title="Skua">
       skuas
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      engage in
      <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">
       kleptoparasitism
      </a>
      , stealing food items from other birds. Kleptoparasitism is thought to be a supplement to food obtained by hunting, rather than a significant part of any species' diet; a study of
      <a href="/wiki/Great_frigatebird" title="Great frigatebird">
       great frigatebirds
      </a>
      stealing from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masked_Booby" title="Masked Booby">
       masked boobies
      </a>
      estimated that the frigatebirds stole at most 40% of their food and on average stole only 5%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other birds are
      <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">
       scavengers
      </a>
      ; some of these, like
      <a href="/wiki/Vulture" title="Vulture">
       vultures
      </a>
      , are specialised carrion eaters, while others, like gulls,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corvid" title="Corvid">
       corvids
      </a>
      , or other birds of prey, are opportunists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Water_and_drinking">
       Water and drinking
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Water is needed by many birds although their mode of excretion and lack of
      <a href="/wiki/Sweat_gland" title="Sweat gland">
       sweat glands
      </a>
      reduces the physiological demands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some desert birds can obtain their water needs entirely from moisture in their food. They may also have other adaptations such as allowing their body temperature to rise, saving on moisture loss from evaporative cooling or panting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Seabirds can drink seawater and have
      <a href="/wiki/Salt_gland" title="Salt gland">
       salt glands
      </a>
      inside the head that eliminate excess salt out of the nostrils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most birds scoop water in their beaks and raise their head to let water run down the throat. Some species, especially of arid zones, belonging to the
      <a href="/wiki/Columbidae" title="Columbidae">
       pigeon
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estrildidae" title="Estrildidae">
       finch
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coliidae" title="Coliidae">
       mousebird
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turnicidae" title="Turnicidae">
       button-quail
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otididae" title="Otididae">
       bustard
      </a>
      families are capable of sucking up water without the need to tilt back their heads.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some desert birds depend on water sources and
      <a href="/wiki/Sandgrouse" title="Sandgrouse">
       sandgrouse
      </a>
      are particularly well known for their daily congregations at waterholes. Nesting sandgrouse and many plovers carry water to their young by wetting their belly feathers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some birds carry water for chicks at the nest in their crop or regurgitate it along with food. The pigeon family, flamingos and penguins have adaptations to produce a nutritive fluid called
      <a href="/wiki/Crop_milk" title="Crop milk">
       crop milk
      </a>
      that they provide to their chicks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Feather_care">
       Feather care
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Feathers being critical to the survival of a bird, require maintenance. Apart from physical wear and tear, feathers face the onslaught of fungi, ectoparasitic feather mites and
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_louse" title="Bird louse">
       birdlice
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The physical condition of feathers are maintained by preening often with the application of secretions from the preen gland. Birds also bathe in water or dust themselves. While some birds dip into shallow water, more aerial species may make aerial dips into water and arboreal species often make use of dew or rain that collect on leaves. Birds of arid regions make use of loose soil to dust-bathe. A behaviour termed as
      <a href="/wiki/Anting_(bird_activity)" title="Anting (bird activity)">
       anting
      </a>
      in which the bird encourages ants to run through their plumage is also thought to help them reduce the ectoparasite load in feathers. Many species will spread out their wings and expose them to direct sunlight and this too is thought to help in reducing fungal and ectoparasitic activity that may lead to feather damage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">
       Migration
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">
       Bird migration
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Many bird species migrate to take advantage of global differences of
      <a href="/wiki/Season" title="Season">
       seasonal
      </a>
      temperatures, therefore optimising availability of food sources and breeding habitat. These migrations vary among the different groups. Many landbirds,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shorebird" title="Shorebird">
       shorebirds
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterbird" title="Waterbird">
       waterbirds
      </a>
      undertake annual long distance migrations, usually triggered by the length of daylight as well as weather conditions. These birds are characterised by a breeding season spent in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">
       temperate
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">
       polar regions
      </a>
      and a non-breeding season in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical">
       tropical
      </a>
      regions or opposite hemisphere. Before migration, birds substantially increase body fats and reserves and reduce the size of some of their organs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battley_70-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Battley-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klaassen_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Klaassen-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Migration is highly demanding energetically, particularly as birds need to cross deserts and oceans without refuelling. Landbirds have a flight range of around 2,500km (1,600mi) and shorebirds can fly up to 4,000km (2,500mi),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_130-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although the
      <a href="/wiki/Bar-tailed_godwit" title="Bar-tailed godwit">
       bar-tailed godwit
      </a>
      is capable of non-stop flights of up to 10,200km (6,300mi).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">
       Seabirds
      </a>
      also undertake long migrations, the longest annual migration being those of
      <a href="/wiki/Sooty_shearwater" title="Sooty shearwater">
       sooty shearwaters
      </a>
      , which nest in
      <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">
       New Zealand
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">
       Chile
      </a>
      and spend the northern summer feeding in the North Pacific off Japan,
      <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">
       Alaska
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">
       California
      </a>
      , an annual round trip of 64,000km (39,800mi).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other seabirds disperse after breeding, travelling widely but having no set migration route. Albatrosses nesting in the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">
       Southern Ocean
      </a>
      often undertake circumpolar trips between breeding seasons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bar-tailed_Godwit_migration.jpg">
        <img alt=" A map of the Pacific Ocean with several coloured lines representing bird routes running from New Zealand to Korea" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="416" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Bar-tailed_Godwit_migration.jpg/220px-Bar-tailed_Godwit_migration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Bar-tailed_Godwit_migration.jpg/330px-Bar-tailed_Godwit_migration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Bar-tailed_Godwit_migration.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bar-tailed_Godwit_migration.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The routes of satellite-tagged
        <a href="/wiki/Bar-tailed_godwit" title="Bar-tailed godwit">
         bar-tailed godwits
        </a>
        migrating north from
        <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">
         New Zealand
        </a>
        . This species has the longest known non-stop migration of any species, up to 10,200km (6,300mi).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Some bird species undertake shorter migrations, travelling only as far as is required to avoid bad weather or obtain food.
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/irruptive" title="wiktionary:irruptive">
       Irruptive
      </a>
      species such as the boreal
      <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">
       finches
      </a>
      are one such group and can commonly be found at a location in one year and absent the next. This type of migration is normally associated with food availability.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Species may also travel shorter distances over part of their range, with individuals from higher latitudes travelling into the existing range of conspecifics; others undertake partial migrations, where only a fraction of the population, usually females and subdominant males, migrates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Partial migration can form a large percentage of the migration behaviour of birds in some regions; in Australia, surveys found that 44% of non-passerine birds and 32% of passerines were partially migratory.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Altitudinal_migration" title="Altitudinal migration">
       Altitudinal migration
      </a>
      is a form of short distance migration in which birds spend the breeding season at higher altitudes elevations and move to lower ones during suboptimal conditions. It is most often triggered by temperature changes and usually occurs when
      <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">
       the normal territories
      </a>
      also become inhospitable due to lack of food.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some species may also be nomadic, holding no fixed territory and moving according to weather and food availability.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/True_parrots" title="True parrots">
       Parrots
      </a>
      as a
      <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">
       family
      </a>
      are overwhelmingly neither migratory nor sedentary but considered to either be dispersive, irruptive, nomadic or undertake small and irregular migrations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The ability of birds to return to precise locations across vast distances has been known for some time; in an experiment conducted in the 1950s a
      <a href="/wiki/Manx_shearwater" title="Manx shearwater">
       Manx shearwater
      </a>
      released in
      <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">
       Boston
      </a>
      returned to its colony in
      <a href="/wiki/Skomer" title="Skomer">
       Skomer
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
       Wales
      </a>
      , within 13 days, a distance of 5,150km (3,200mi).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Birds navigate during migration using a variety of methods. For
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diurnal_animal" title="Diurnal animal">
       diurnal
      </a>
      migrants, the
      <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
       sun
      </a>
      is used to navigate by day, and a stellar compass is used at night. Birds that use the sun compensate for the changing position of the sun during the day by the use of an
      <a href="/wiki/Chronobiology" title="Chronobiology">
       internal clock
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Orientation with the stellar compass depends on the position of the
      <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">
       constellations
      </a>
      surrounding
      <a href="/wiki/Polaris" title="Polaris">
       Polaris
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These are backed up in some species by their ability to sense the Earth's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geomagnetism" title="Geomagnetism">
       geomagnetism
      </a>
      through specialised
      <a href="/wiki/Photoreceptor_cell" title="Photoreceptor cell">
       photoreceptors
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">
       Communication
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">
       Bird vocalization
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stavenn_Eurypiga_helias_00.jpg">
        <img alt="Large brown patterned ground bird with outstretched wings each with a large spot in the centre" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="480" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Stavenn_Eurypiga_helias_00.jpg/220px-Stavenn_Eurypiga_helias_00.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Stavenn_Eurypiga_helias_00.jpg/330px-Stavenn_Eurypiga_helias_00.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Stavenn_Eurypiga_helias_00.jpg/440px-Stavenn_Eurypiga_helias_00.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stavenn_Eurypiga_helias_00.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The startling display of the
        <a href="/wiki/Sunbittern" title="Sunbittern">
         sunbittern
        </a>
        mimics a large predator.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Birds
      <a href="/wiki/Animal_communication" title="Animal communication">
       communicate
      </a>
      using primarily visual and auditory signals. Signals can be interspecific (between species) and intraspecific (within species).
     </p>
     <p>
      Birds sometimes use plumage to assess and assert social dominance,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to display breeding condition in sexually selected species, or to make threatening displays, as in the
      <a href="/wiki/Sunbittern" title="Sunbittern">
       sunbittern
      </a>
      's mimicry of a large predator to ward off
      <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">
       hawks
      </a>
      and protect young chicks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Variation in plumage also allows for the identification of birds, particularly between species. Visual communication among birds may also involve ritualised displays, which have developed from non-signalling actions such as preening, the adjustments of feather position, pecking, or other behaviour. These displays may signal aggression or submission or may contribute to the formation of pair-bonds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most elaborate displays occur during courtship, where "dances" are often formed from complex combinations of many possible component movements;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      males' breeding success may depend on the quality of such displays.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Troglodytes_aedon_-_House_Wren_-_XC79974.ogg" title="File:Troglodytes aedon - House Wren - XC79974.ogg">
           Bird song
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>
          <div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px">

             download the clip
            </a>
            or
            <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">
             download a player
            </a>
            to play the clip in your browser.
           </audio>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
          Song of the
          <a href="/wiki/House_wren" title="House wren">
           house wren
          </a>
          , a common North American songbird
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <span>
         <i>
          Problems playing this file? See
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
           media help
          </a>
          .
         </i>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">
       Bird calls and songs
      </a>
      , which are produced in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrinx_(biology)" title="Syrinx (biology)">
       syrinx
      </a>
      , are the major means by which birds communicate with
      <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">
       sound
      </a>
      . This communication can be very complex; some species can operate the two sides of the syrinx independently, allowing the simultaneous production of two different songs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suthers_72-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Suthers-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Calls are used for a variety of purposes, including mate attraction,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      evaluation of potential mates,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      bond formation, the claiming and maintenance of territories,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the identification of other individuals (such as when parents look for chicks in colonies or when mates reunite at the start of breeding season),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the warning of other birds of potential predators, sometimes with specific information about the nature of the threat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some birds also use mechanical sounds for auditory communication. The
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenocorypha" title="Coenocorypha">
        Coenocorypha
       </a>
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Snipe" title="Snipe">
       snipes
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">
       New Zealand
      </a>
      drive air through their feathers,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miskelly_149-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Miskelly-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">
       woodpeckers
      </a>
      drum territorially,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Attenborough_69-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Attenborough-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Palm_cockatoo" title="Palm cockatoo">
       palm cockatoos
      </a>
      use tools to drum.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red-billed_quelea_flocking_at_waterhole.jpg">
        <img alt=" massive flock of tiny birds seen from distance so that birds appear as specks" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Red-billed_quelea_flocking_at_waterhole.jpg/220px-Red-billed_quelea_flocking_at_waterhole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Red-billed_quelea_flocking_at_waterhole.jpg/330px-Red-billed_quelea_flocking_at_waterhole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Red-billed_quelea_flocking_at_waterhole.jpg/440px-Red-billed_quelea_flocking_at_waterhole.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red-billed_quelea_flocking_at_waterhole.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_quelea" title="Red-billed quelea">
         Red-billed queleas
        </a>
        , the most numerous species of bird,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flycatcher_151-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-flycatcher-151">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          151
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        form enormous flockssometimes tens of thousands strong.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Flocking_and_other_associations">
       Flocking and other associations
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      While some birds are essentially territorial or live in small family groups, other birds may form large
      <a href="/wiki/Flock_(birds)" title="Flock (birds)">
       flocks
      </a>
      . The principal benefits of flocking are
      <a href="/wiki/Safety_in_numbers" title="Safety in numbers">
       safety in numbers
      </a>
      and increased foraging efficiency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Defence against predators is particularly important in closed habitats like forests, where
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambush_predation" title="Ambush predation">
       ambush predation
      </a>
      is common and multiple eyes can provide a valuable early warning system. This has led to the development of many
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed-species_feeding_flock" title="Mixed-species feeding flock">
       mixed-species feeding flocks
      </a>
      , which are usually composed of small numbers of many species; these flocks provide safety in numbers but increase potential competition for resources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Costs of flocking include bullying of socially subordinate birds by more dominant birds and the reduction of feeding efficiency in certain cases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
       <a href="#cite_note-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Birds sometimes also form associations with non-avian species. Plunge-diving
      <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">
       seabirds
      </a>
      associate with
      <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">
       dolphins
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">
       tuna
      </a>
      , which push shoaling fish towards the surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AU_154-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AU-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hornbills have a
      <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">
       mutualistic relationship
      </a>
      with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwarf_mongoose" title="Dwarf mongoose">
       dwarf mongooses
      </a>
      , in which they forage together and warn each other of nearby
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" title="Birds of prey">
       birds of prey
      </a>
      and other predators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Resting_and_roosting">
       Resting and roosting
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caribbean_Flamingo2_(Phoenicopterus_ruber)_(0424)_-_Relic38.jpg">
        <img alt="Pink flamingo with grey legs and long neck pressed against body and head tucked under wings" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2460" data-file-width="3120" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Caribbean_Flamingo2_%28Phoenicopterus_ruber%29_%280424%29_-_Relic38.jpg/220px-Caribbean_Flamingo2_%28Phoenicopterus_ruber%29_%280424%29_-_Relic38.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Caribbean_Flamingo2_%28Phoenicopterus_ruber%29_%280424%29_-_Relic38.jpg/330px-Caribbean_Flamingo2_%28Phoenicopterus_ruber%29_%280424%29_-_Relic38.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Caribbean_Flamingo2_%28Phoenicopterus_ruber%29_%280424%29_-_Relic38.jpg/440px-Caribbean_Flamingo2_%28Phoenicopterus_ruber%29_%280424%29_-_Relic38.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caribbean_Flamingo2_(Phoenicopterus_ruber)_(0424)_-_Relic38.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Many birds, like this
        <a href="/wiki/American_flamingo" title="American flamingo">
         American flamingo
        </a>
        , tuck their head into their back when sleeping
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The high metabolic rates of birds during the active part of the day is supplemented by rest at other times. Sleeping birds often use a type of sleep known as vigilant sleep, where periods of rest are interspersed with quick eye-opening "peeks", allowing them to be sensitive to disturbances and enable rapid escape from threats.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
       <a href="#cite_note-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Swift" title="Swift">
       Swifts
      </a>
      are believed to be able to sleep in flight and radar observations suggest that they orient themselves to face the wind in their roosting flight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
       <a href="#cite_note-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been suggested that there may be certain kinds of sleep which are possible even when in flight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
       <a href="#cite_note-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some birds have also demonstrated the capacity to fall into
      <a href="/wiki/Slow-wave_sleep" title="Slow-wave sleep">
       slow-wave sleep
      </a>
      one
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemisphere" title="Cerebral hemisphere">
       hemisphere
      </a>
      of the brain at a time. The birds tend to exercise this ability depending upon its position relative to the outside of the flock. This may allow the eye opposite the sleeping hemisphere to remain vigilant for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">
       predators
      </a>
      by viewing the outer margins of the flock. This adaptation is also known from
      <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">
       marine mammals
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
       <a href="#cite_note-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Communal roosting is common because it lowers the
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">
       loss of body heat
      </a>
      and decreases the risks associated with predators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
       <a href="#cite_note-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Roosting sites are often chosen with regard to thermoregulation and safety.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many sleeping birds bend their heads over their backs and tuck their
      <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">
       bills
      </a>
      in their back feathers, although others place their beaks among their breast feathers. Many birds rest on one leg, while some may pull up their legs into their feathers, especially in cold weather.
      <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">
       Perching birds
      </a>
      have a tendon locking mechanism that helps them hold on to the perch when they are asleep. Many ground birds, such as quails and pheasants, roost in trees. A few parrots of the genus
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loriculus" title="Loriculus">
        Loriculus
       </a>
      </i>
      roost hanging upside down.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
       <a href="#cite_note-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some
      <a href="/wiki/Hummingbird" title="Hummingbird">
       hummingbirds
      </a>
      go into a nightly state of
      <a href="/wiki/Torpor" title="Torpor">
       torpor
      </a>
      accompanied with a reduction of their metabolic rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
       <a href="#cite_note-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This
      <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">
       physiological adaptation
      </a>
      shows in nearly a hundred other species, including
      <a href="/wiki/Owlet-nightjar" title="Owlet-nightjar">
       owlet-nightjars
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nightjar" title="Nightjar">
       nightjars
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Woodswallow" title="Woodswallow">
       woodswallows
      </a>
      . One species, the
      <a href="/wiki/Common_poorwill" title="Common poorwill">
       common poorwill
      </a>
      , even enters a state of
      <a href="/wiki/Hibernation" title="Hibernation">
       hibernation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
       <a href="#cite_note-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Birds do not have sweat glands, but they may cool themselves by moving to shade, standing in water, panting, increasing their surface area, fluttering their throat or by using special behaviours like
      <a href="/wiki/Urohidrosis" title="Urohidrosis">
       urohidrosis
      </a>
      to cool themselves.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">
       Breeding
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Main category:
      <a href="/wiki/Category:Avian_sexuality" title="Category:Avian sexuality">
       Avian sexuality
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Animal_sexual_behaviour#Birds" title="Animal sexual behaviour">
       Animal sexual behaviour  Birds
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Seabird_breeding_behavior" title="Seabird breeding behavior">
       Seabird breeding behavior
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection_in_birds" title="Sexual selection in birds">
       Sexual selection in birds
      </a>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Social_systems">
       Social systems
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raggiana_Bird-of-Paradise_wild_5.jpg">
        <img alt=" Bird faces up with green face, black breast and pink lower body. Elaborate long feathers on the wings and tail." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Raggiana_Bird-of-Paradise_wild_5.jpg/220px-Raggiana_Bird-of-Paradise_wild_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Raggiana_Bird-of-Paradise_wild_5.jpg/330px-Raggiana_Bird-of-Paradise_wild_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Raggiana_Bird-of-Paradise_wild_5.jpg/440px-Raggiana_Bird-of-Paradise_wild_5.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raggiana_Bird-of-Paradise_wild_5.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Like others of its family the male
        <a href="/wiki/Raggiana_bird-of-paradise" title="Raggiana bird-of-paradise">
         Raggiana bird-of-paradise
        </a>
        has elaborate breeding plumage used to impress females.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
         <a href="#cite_note-165">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          165
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Ninety-five percent of bird species are socially monogamous. These species pair for at least the length of the breeding season orin some casesfor several years or until the death of one mate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Monogamy allows for both
      <a href="/wiki/Paternal_care" title="Paternal care">
       paternal care
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Parental_investment" title="Parental investment">
       biparental care
      </a>
      , which is especially important for species in which females require males' assistance for successful brood-rearing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Among many socially monogamous species, extra-pair copulation (infidelity) is common.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such behaviour typically occurs between dominant males and females paired with subordinate males, but may also be the result of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forced_copulation" title="Forced copulation">
       forced copulation
      </a>
      in ducks and other
      <a href="/wiki/Anatidae" title="Anatidae">
       anatids
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Female birds have
      <a href="/wiki/Female_sperm_storage" title="Female sperm storage">
       sperm storage
      </a>
      mechanisms that allow sperm from to remain viable long after copulation, a hundred days in some species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
       <a href="#cite_note-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sperm from multiple males may
      <a href="/wiki/Sperm_competition" title="Sperm competition">
       compete
      </a>
      through this mechanism. For females, possible benefits of extra-pair copulation include getting better genes for her offspring and insuring against the possibility of infertility in her mate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
       <a href="#cite_note-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Males of species that engage in extra-pair copulations will closely guard their mates to ensure the parentage of the offspring that they raise.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
       <a href="#cite_note-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other mating systems, including
      <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">
       polygyny
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">
       polyandry
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">
       polygamy
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Polygynandry" title="Polygynandry">
       polygynandry
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Promiscuity" title="Promiscuity">
       promiscuity
      </a>
      , also occur.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Polygamous breeding systems arise when females are able to raise broods without the help of males.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some species may use more than one system depending on the circumstances.
     </p>
     <p>
      Breeding usually involves some form of courtship display, typically performed by the male.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most displays are rather simple and involve some type of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bird_song" title="Bird song">
       song
      </a>
      . Some displays, however, are quite elaborate. Depending on the species, these may include wing or tail drumming, dancing, aerial flights, or communal
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lek_(mating_arena)" title="Lek (mating arena)">
       lekking
      </a>
      . Females are generally the ones that drive partner selection,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although in the polyandrous
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalaropes" title="Phalaropes">
       phalaropes
      </a>
      , this is reversed: plainer males choose brightly coloured females.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courtship_feeding" title="Courtship feeding">
       Courtship feeding
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billing_(birds)" title="Billing (birds)">
       billing
      </a>
      and allopreening are commonly performed between partners, generally after the birds have paired and mated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Attenborough_69-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Attenborough-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homosexuality_in_animals#Birds" title="Homosexuality in animals">
       Homosexual behaviour has been observed
      </a>
      in males or females in numerous species of birds, including copulation, pair-bonding, and joint parenting of chicks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
       <a href="#cite_note-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Territories.2C_nesting_and_incubation">
       Territories, nesting and incubation
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">
       Bird nest
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Many birds actively defend a territory from others of the same species during the breeding season; maintenance of territories protects the food source for their chicks. Species that are unable to defend feeding territories, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">
       seabirds
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Swift" title="Swift">
       swifts
      </a>
      , often breed in
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">
       colonies
      </a>
      instead; this is thought to offer protection from predators. Colonial breeders defend small nesting sites, and competition between and within species for nesting sites can be intense.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      All birds lay
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amniotic_egg" title="Amniotic egg">
       amniotic eggs
      </a>
      with hard shells made mostly of
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">
       calcium carbonate
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hole and burrow nesting species tend to lay white or pale eggs, while open nesters lay
      <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">
       camouflaged
      </a>
      eggs. There are many exceptions to this pattern, however; the ground-nesting
      <a href="/wiki/Nightjar" title="Nightjar">
       nightjars
      </a>
      have pale eggs, and camouflage is instead provided by their plumage. Species that are victims of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brood_parasites" title="Brood parasites">
       brood parasites
      </a>
      have varying egg colours to improve the chances of spotting a parasite's egg, which forces female parasites to match their eggs to those of their hosts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Golden-backed_Weaver.jpg">
        <img alt="Yellow weaver (bird) with black head hangs an upside-down nest woven out of grass fronds." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="1024" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Golden-backed_Weaver.jpg/220px-Golden-backed_Weaver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Golden-backed_Weaver.jpg/330px-Golden-backed_Weaver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Golden-backed_Weaver.jpg/440px-Golden-backed_Weaver.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Golden-backed_Weaver.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Male
        <a href="/wiki/Golden-backed_weaver" title="Golden-backed weaver">
         golden-backed weavers
        </a>
        construct elaborate suspended nests out of grass.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Bird eggs are usually laid in a
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">
       nest
      </a>
      . Most species create somewhat elaborate nests, which can be cups, domes, plates, beds scrapes, mounds, or burrows.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansell_179-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hansell-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some bird nests, however, are extremely primitive;
      <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">
       albatross
      </a>
      nests are no more than a scrape on the ground. Most birds build nests in sheltered, hidden areas to avoid predation, but large or colonial birdswhich are more capable of defencemay build more open nests. During nest construction, some species seek out plant matter from plants with parasite-reducing toxins to improve chick survival,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and feathers are often used for nest insulation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansell_179-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hansell-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some bird species have no nests; the cliff-nesting
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_guillemot" title="Common guillemot">
       common guillemot
      </a>
      lays its eggs on bare rock, and male
      <a href="/wiki/Emperor_penguin" title="Emperor penguin">
       emperor penguins
      </a>
      keep eggs between their body and feet. The absence of nests is especially prevalent in ground-nesting species where the newly hatched young are
      <a href="/wiki/Precocial" title="Precocial">
       precocial
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Phoebe-nest-Brown-headed-Cowbird-egg.jpg">
        <img alt=" Nest made of straw with five white eggs and one grey speckled egg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Eastern_Phoebe-nest-Brown-headed-Cowbird-egg.jpg/220px-Eastern_Phoebe-nest-Brown-headed-Cowbird-egg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Eastern_Phoebe-nest-Brown-headed-Cowbird-egg.jpg/330px-Eastern_Phoebe-nest-Brown-headed-Cowbird-egg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Eastern_Phoebe-nest-Brown-headed-Cowbird-egg.jpg/440px-Eastern_Phoebe-nest-Brown-headed-Cowbird-egg.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Phoebe-nest-Brown-headed-Cowbird-egg.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Nest of an
        <a href="/wiki/Eastern_phoebe" title="Eastern phoebe">
         eastern phoebe
        </a>
        that has been parasitised by a
        <a href="/wiki/Brown-headed_cowbird" title="Brown-headed cowbird">
         brown-headed cowbird
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Incubation, which optimises temperature for chick development, usually begins after the last egg has been laid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In monogamous species incubation duties are often shared, whereas in polygamous species one parent is wholly responsible for incubation. Warmth from parents passes to the eggs through
      <a href="/wiki/Brood_patch" title="Brood patch">
       brood patches
      </a>
      , areas of bare skin on the abdomen or breast of the incubating birds. Incubation can be an energetically demanding process; adult albatrosses, for instance, lose as much as 83 grams (2.9oz) of body weight per day of incubation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
       <a href="#cite_note-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The warmth for the incubation of the eggs of
      <a href="/wiki/Megapode" title="Megapode">
       megapodes
      </a>
      comes from the sun, decaying vegetation or volcanic sources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
       <a href="#cite_note-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Incubation periods range from 10 days (in
      <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">
       woodpeckers
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cuckoo" title="Cuckoo">
       cuckoos
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">
       passerine
      </a>
      birds) to over 80 days (in albatrosses and
      <a href="/wiki/Kiwi" title="Kiwi">
       kiwis
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The diversity of characteristics of birds is great, sometimes even in closely related species. Several avian characteristics are compared in the table below.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnAge_108-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AnAge-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_109-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ADW-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:center">
      <tr>

         (days)
        </p>
       </th>

        </p>

       </td>

       </td>

       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr style="background:#dfd;">
       <td>
        Greater roadrunner (
        <i>
         Geococcyx californianus
        </i>
        )
       </td>
       <td>
        376
       </td>
       <td>
        20
       </td>
       <td>
        1.5
       </td>
       <td>
        4
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr style="background:#dfd;">

        <i>

        </i>
        )
       </td>
       <td>
        2,200
       </td>
       <td>

       </td>
