     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Parental_care_and_fledging">
       Parental care and fledging
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Parental_care_in_birds" title="Parental care in birds">
       Parental care in birds
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      At the time of their hatching, chicks range in development from helpless to independent, depending on their species. Helpless chicks are termed
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Altricial" title="Altricial">
        altricial
       </a>
      </i>
      , and tend to be born small,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blindness" title="Blindness">
       blind
      </a>
      , immobile and naked; chicks that are mobile and feathered upon hatching are termed
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Precocial" title="Precocial">
        precocial
       </a>
      </i>
      . Altricial chicks need help
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">
       thermoregulating
      </a>
      and must be brooded for longer than precocial chicks. Chicks at neither of these extremes can be semi-precocial or semi-altricial.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calliope-nest_edit.jpg">
        <img alt="Hummingbird perched on edge of tiny nest places food into mouth of one of two chicks" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1365" data-file-width="2048" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Calliope-nest_edit.jpg/220px-Calliope-nest_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Calliope-nest_edit.jpg/330px-Calliope-nest_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Calliope-nest_edit.jpg/440px-Calliope-nest_edit.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calliope-nest_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A female
        <a href="/wiki/Calliope_hummingbird" title="Calliope hummingbird">
         Calliope hummingbird
        </a>
        feeding fully grown chicks.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White-breasted_Woodswallow_chicks_in_nest.jpg">
        <img alt="Looking down on three helpless blind chicks in a nest within the hollow of a dead tree trunk" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/White-breasted_Woodswallow_chicks_in_nest.jpg/220px-White-breasted_Woodswallow_chicks_in_nest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/White-breasted_Woodswallow_chicks_in_nest.jpg/330px-White-breasted_Woodswallow_chicks_in_nest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/White-breasted_Woodswallow_chicks_in_nest.jpg/440px-White-breasted_Woodswallow_chicks_in_nest.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:White-breasted_Woodswallow_chicks_in_nest.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Altricial" title="Altricial">
         Altricial
        </a>
        chicks of a
        <a href="/wiki/White-breasted_woodswallow" title="White-breasted woodswallow">
         white-breasted woodswallow
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The length and nature of parental care varies widely amongst different orders and species. At one extreme, parental care in
      <a href="/wiki/Megapode" title="Megapode">
       megapodes
      </a>
      ends at hatching; the newly hatched chick digs itself out of the nest mound without parental assistance and can fend for itself immediately.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the other extreme, many seabirds have extended periods of parental care, the longest being that of the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_frigatebird" title="Great frigatebird">
       great frigatebird
      </a>
      , whose chicks take up to six months to
      <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">
       fledge
      </a>
      and are fed by the parents for up to an additional 14 months.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
       <a href="#cite_note-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <i>
       chick guard stage
      </i>
      describes the period of breeding during which one of the adult birds is permanently present at the nest after chicks have hatched. The main purpose of the guard stage is to aid offspring to thermoregulate and protect them from predation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In some species, both parents care for nestlings and fledglings; in others, such care is the responsibility of only one sex. In some species,
      <a href="/wiki/Helpers_at_the_nest" title="Helpers at the nest">
       other members
      </a>
      of the same speciesusually close relatives of the
      <a href="/wiki/Breeding_pair" title="Breeding pair">
       breeding pair
      </a>
      , such as offspring from previous broodswill help with the raising of the young.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such alloparenting is particularly common among the
      <a href="/wiki/Corvida" title="Corvida">
       Corvida
      </a>
      , which includes such birds as the true
      <a href="/wiki/Corvidae" title="Corvidae">
       crows
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Australian_magpie" title="Australian magpie">
       Australian magpie
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fairy-wren" title="Fairy-wren">
       fairy-wrens
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
       <a href="#cite_note-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        187
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but has been observed in species as different as the
      <a href="/wiki/Rifleman_(bird)" title="Rifleman (bird)">
       rifleman
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">
       red kite
      </a>
      . Among most groups of animals,
      <a href="/wiki/Paternal_care" title="Paternal care">
       male parental care
      </a>
      is rare. In birds, however, it is quite commonmore so than in any other vertebrate class.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gill_55-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gill-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though territory and nest site defence, incubation, and chick feeding are often shared tasks, there is sometimes a
      <a href="/wiki/Division_of_labour" title="Division of labour">
       division of labour
      </a>
      in which one mate undertakes all or most of a particular duty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
       <a href="#cite_note-188">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        188
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The point at which chicks
      <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">
       fledge
      </a>
      varies dramatically. The chicks of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Synthliboramphus" title="Synthliboramphus">
        Synthliboramphus
       </a>
      </i>
      murrelets, like the
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_murrelet" title="Ancient murrelet">
       ancient murrelet
      </a>
      , leave the nest the night after they hatch, following their parents out to sea, where they are raised away from terrestrial predators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
       <a href="#cite_note-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some other species, such as ducks, move their chicks away from the nest at an early age. In most species, chicks leave the nest just before, or soon after, they are able to fly. The amount of parental care after fledging varies; albatross chicks leave the nest on their own and receive no further help, while other species continue some supplementary feeding after fledging.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
       <a href="#cite_note-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Chicks may also follow their parents during their first
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">
       migration
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
       <a href="#cite_note-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Brood_parasites">
       Brood parasites
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">
       Brood parasite
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reed_warbler_cuckoo.jpg">
        <img alt="Small brown bird places an insect in the bill of much larger grey bird in nest" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="446" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Reed_warbler_cuckoo.jpg/170px-Reed_warbler_cuckoo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Reed_warbler_cuckoo.jpg/255px-Reed_warbler_cuckoo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Reed_warbler_cuckoo.jpg/340px-Reed_warbler_cuckoo.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reed_warbler_cuckoo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reed_warbler" title="Reed warbler">
         Reed warbler
        </a>
        raising a
        <a href="/wiki/Common_cuckoo" title="Common cuckoo">
         common cuckoo
        </a>
        , a
        <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">
         brood parasite
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brood_parasitism" title="Brood parasitism">
       Brood parasitism
      </a>
      , in which an egg-layer leaves her eggs with another individual's brood, is more common among birds than any other type of organism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brood_192-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-brood-192">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        192
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After a parasitic bird lays her eggs in another bird's nest, they are often accepted and raised by the host at the expense of the host's own brood. Brood parasites may be either
      <i>
       obligate brood parasites
      </i>
      , which must lay their eggs in the nests of other species because they are incapable of raising their own young, or
      <i>
       non-obligate brood parasites
      </i>
      , which sometimes lay eggs in the nests of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspecific" title="Conspecific">
       conspecifics
      </a>
      to increase their reproductive output even though they could have raised their own young.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
       <a href="#cite_note-193">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        193
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One hundred bird species, including
      <a href="/wiki/Honeyguide" title="Honeyguide">
       honeyguides
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Icterid" title="Icterid">
       icterids
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Black-headed_duck" title="Black-headed duck">
       ducks
      </a>
      , are obligate parasites, though the most famous are the
      <a href="/wiki/Cuckoo" title="Cuckoo">
       cuckoos
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brood_192-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-brood-192">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        192
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some brood parasites are adapted to hatch before their host's young, which allows them to destroy the host's eggs by pushing them out of the nest or to kill the host's chicks; this ensures that all food brought to the nest will be fed to the parasitic chicks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
       <a href="#cite_note-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">
       Ecology
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Birds occupy a wide range of ecological positions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flycatcher_151-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-flycatcher-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While some birds are generalists, others are highly specialised in their habitat or food requirements. Even within a single habitat, such as a forest, the
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">
       niches
      </a>
      occupied by different species of birds vary, with some species feeding in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_canopy" title="Forest canopy">
       forest canopy
      </a>
      , others beneath the canopy, and still others on the forest floor. Forest birds may be
      <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">
       insectivores
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Frugivore" title="Frugivore">
       frugivores
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Nectarivore" title="Nectarivore">
       nectarivores
      </a>
      . Aquatic birds generally feed by fishing, plant eating, and piracy or
      <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">
       kleptoparasitism
      </a>
      . Birds of prey specialise in hunting mammals or other birds, while vultures are specialised
      <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">
       scavengers
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Avivore" title="Avivore">
       Avivores
      </a>
      are animals that are specialized at preying on birds.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some nectar-feeding birds are important pollinators, and many frugivores play a key role in seed dispersal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clout_195-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clout-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Plants and pollinating birds often
      <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">
       coevolve
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
       <a href="#cite_note-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and in some cases a flower's primary pollinator is the only species capable of reaching its nectar.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
       <a href="#cite_note-197">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Birds are often important to island ecology. Birds have frequently reached islands that mammals have not; on those islands, birds may fulfill ecological roles typically played by larger animals. For example, in New Zealand the
      <a href="/wiki/Moa" title="Moa">
       moas
      </a>
      were important browsers, as are the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_pigeon" title="New Zealand pigeon">
       kereru
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kokako" title="Kokako">
       kokako
      </a>
      today.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clout_195-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Clout-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Today the plants of New Zealand retain the defensive adaptations evolved to protect them from the extinct moa.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
       <a href="#cite_note-198">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        198
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nesting
      <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">
       seabirds
      </a>
      may also affect the ecology of islands and surrounding seas, principally through the concentration of large quantities of
      <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">
       guano
      </a>
      , which may enrich the local soil
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
       <a href="#cite_note-199">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        199
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the surrounding seas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
       <a href="#cite_note-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A wide variety of
      <a href="/wiki/Avian_ecology_field_methods" title="Avian ecology field methods">
       avian ecology field methods
      </a>
      , including counts, nest monitoring, and capturing and marking, are used for researching avian ecology.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">
       Relationship with humans
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Industrial-Chicken-Coop.JPG">
        <img alt=" Two rows of cages in a dark barn with many white chickens in each cage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Industrial-Chicken-Coop.JPG/220px-Industrial-Chicken-Coop.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Industrial-Chicken-Coop.JPG/330px-Industrial-Chicken-Coop.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Industrial-Chicken-Coop.JPG/440px-Industrial-Chicken-Coop.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Industrial-Chicken-Coop.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_farming" title="Industrial farming">
         Industrial farming
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">
         chickens
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Since birds are highly visible and common animals, humans have had a relationship with them since the dawn of man.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
       <a href="#cite_note-201">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        201
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sometimes, these relationships are
      <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">
       mutualistic
      </a>
      , like the cooperative honey-gathering among
      <a href="/wiki/Honeyguide" title="Honeyguide">
       honeyguides
      </a>
      and African peoples such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borana_people" title="Borana people">
       Borana
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
       <a href="#cite_note-202">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        202
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other times, they may be
      <a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">
       commensal
      </a>
      , as when species such as the
      <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">
       house sparrow
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203">
       <a href="#cite_note-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        203
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      have benefited from human activities. Several bird species have become commercially significant agricultural pests,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
       <a href="#cite_note-204">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        204
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and some pose an
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_strike" title="Bird strike">
       aviation hazard
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
       <a href="#cite_note-205">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        205
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Human activities can also be detrimental, and have threatened numerous bird species with extinction (
      <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">
       hunting
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avian_lead_poisoning" title="Avian lead poisoning">
       avian lead poisoning
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">
       pesticides
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">
       roadkill
      </a>
      , and predation by pet
      <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">
       cats
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">
       dogs
      </a>
      are common sources of death for birds).
     </p>
     <p>
      Birds can act as vectors for spreading diseases such as
      <a href="/wiki/Psittacosis" title="Psittacosis">
       psittacosis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Salmonellosis" title="Salmonellosis">
       salmonellosis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Campylobacteriosis" title="Campylobacteriosis">
       campylobacteriosis
      </a>
      , mycobacteriosis (avian
      <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">
       tuberculosis
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Avian_influenza" title="Avian influenza">
       avian influenza
      </a>
      (bird flu),
      <a href="/wiki/Giardiasis" title="Giardiasis">
       giardiasis
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Cryptosporidiosis" title="Cryptosporidiosis">
       cryptosporidiosis
      </a>
      over long distances. Some of these are
      <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">
       zoonotic diseases
      </a>
      that can also be transmitted to humans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206">
       <a href="#cite_note-206">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        206
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_importance">
       Economic importance
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Pet#Birds" title="Pet">
       Pet  Birds
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Domesticated birds raised for meat and eggs, called
      <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">
       poultry
      </a>
      , are the largest source of animal protein eaten by humans; in 2003,
      <span class="nowrap">
       76 million
      </span>
      tons of poultry and
      <span class="nowrap">
       61 million
      </span>
      tons of eggs were produced worldwide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
       <a href="#cite_note-207">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        207
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">
       Chickens
      </a>
      account for much of human poultry consumption, though domesticated
      <a href="/wiki/Domesticated_turkey" title="Domesticated turkey">
       turkeys
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Domestic_duck" title="Domestic duck">
       ducks
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Domestic_goose" title="Domestic goose">
       geese
      </a>
      are also relatively common. Many species of birds are also hunted for meat. Bird hunting is primarily a recreational activity except in extremely undeveloped areas. The most important birds hunted in North and South America are waterfowl; other widely hunted birds include
      <a href="/wiki/Pheasant" title="Pheasant">
       pheasants
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Wild_turkey" title="Wild turkey">
       wild turkeys
      </a>
      , quail,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dove" title="Dove">
       doves
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Partridge" title="Partridge">
       partridge
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Grouse" title="Grouse">
       grouse
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Snipe" title="Snipe">
       snipe
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Woodcock" title="Woodcock">
       woodcock
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208">
       <a href="#cite_note-208">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        208
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Muttonbirding" title="Muttonbirding">
       Muttonbirding
      </a>
      is also popular in Australia and New Zealand.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209">
       <a href="#cite_note-209">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        209
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though some hunting, such as that of muttonbirds, may be sustainable, hunting has led to the extinction or endangerment of dozens of species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210">
       <a href="#cite_note-210">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        210
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FishingCormorants.jpg">
        <img alt=" Illustration of fisherman on raft with pole for punting and numerous black birds on raft" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="764" data-file-width="522" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/FishingCormorants.jpg/170px-FishingCormorants.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/FishingCormorants.jpg/255px-FishingCormorants.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/FishingCormorants.jpg/340px-FishingCormorants.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FishingCormorants.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The use of cormorants by Asian fishermen is in steep decline but survives in some areas as a tourist attraction.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Other commercially valuable products from birds include feathers (especially the
      <a href="/wiki/Down_feather" title="Down feather">
       down
      </a>
      of geese and ducks), which are used as insulation in clothing and bedding, and seabird feces (
      <a href="/wiki/Guano" title="Guano">
       guano
      </a>
      ), which is a valuable source of phosphorus and nitrogen. The
      <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Pacific" title="War of the Pacific">
       War of the Pacific
      </a>
      , sometimes called the Guano War, was fought in part over the control of guano deposits.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
       <a href="#cite_note-211">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        211
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Birds have been domesticated by humans both as pets and for practical purposes. Colourful birds, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parrot_(family)" title="Parrot (family)">
       parrots
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Myna" title="Myna">
       mynas
      </a>
      , are bred in
      <a href="/wiki/Aviculture" title="Aviculture">
       captivity
      </a>
      or kept as pets, a practice that has led to the illegal trafficking of some
      <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">
       endangered species
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212">
       <a href="#cite_note-212">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        212
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">
       Falcons
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">
       cormorants
      </a>
      have long been used for
      <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">
       hunting
      </a>
      and fishing, respectively.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messenger_pigeon" title="Messenger pigeon">
       Messenger pigeons
      </a>
      , used since at least 1AD, remained important as recently as
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World WarII
      </a>
      . Today, such activities are more common either as hobbies, for entertainment and tourism,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213">
       <a href="#cite_note-213">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        213
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or for sports such as
      <a href="/wiki/Pigeon_racing" title="Pigeon racing">
       pigeon racing
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Amateur bird enthusiasts (called birdwatchers, twitchers or, more commonly,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birding" title="Birding">
       birders
      </a>
      ) number in the millions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214">
       <a href="#cite_note-214">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many homeowners erect
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_feeder" title="Bird feeder">
       bird feeders
      </a>
      near their homes to attract various species.
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_feeding" title="Bird feeding">
       Bird feeding
      </a>
      has grown into a multimillion dollar industry; for example, an estimated 75% of households in Britain provide food for birds at some point during the winter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
       <a href="#cite_note-215">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        215
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion.2C_folklore_and_culture">
       Religion, folklore and culture
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vogel_Drei_(Meister_der_Spielkarten).jpg">
        <img alt="Woodcut of three long-legged and long-necked birds" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="493" data-file-width="323" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Vogel_Drei_%28Meister_der_Spielkarten%29.jpg/170px-Vogel_Drei_%28Meister_der_Spielkarten%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Vogel_Drei_%28Meister_der_Spielkarten%29.jpg/255px-Vogel_Drei_%28Meister_der_Spielkarten%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Vogel_Drei_%28Meister_der_Spielkarten%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vogel_Drei_(Meister_der_Spielkarten).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        "The 3 of Birds" by the
        <a href="/wiki/Master_of_the_Playing_Cards" title="Master of the Playing Cards">
         Master of the Playing Cards
        </a>
        , 15th-century Germany
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Singing_bird_box_by_Fr%C3%A8res_Rochat,_circa_1810.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4630" data-file-width="3820" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Singing_bird_box_by_Fr%C3%A8res_Rochat%2C_circa_1810.jpg/170px-Singing_bird_box_by_Fr%C3%A8res_Rochat%2C_circa_1810.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Singing_bird_box_by_Fr%C3%A8res_Rochat%2C_circa_1810.jpg/255px-Singing_bird_box_by_Fr%C3%A8res_Rochat%2C_circa_1810.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Singing_bird_box_by_Fr%C3%A8res_Rochat%2C_circa_1810.jpg/340px-Singing_bird_box_by_Fr%C3%A8res_Rochat%2C_circa_1810.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Singing_bird_box_by_Fr%C3%A8res_Rochat,_circa_1810.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A Swiss
        <a href="/wiki/Singing_bird_box" title="Singing bird box">
         singing bird box
        </a>
        , ca. 1810. An example of the interests of human beings in birds, in this case emulating nature with an automaton version.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Birds play prominent and diverse roles in folklore, religion, and
      <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">
       popular culture
      </a>
      . In religion, birds may serve as either messengers or priests and leaders for a
      <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
       deity
      </a>
      , such as in the Cult of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Makemake_(mythology)" title="Makemake (mythology)">
       Makemake
      </a>
      , in which the
      <a href="/wiki/Tangata_manu" title="Tangata manu">
       Tangata manu
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">
       Easter Island
      </a>
      served as chiefs;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
       <a href="#cite_note-216">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        216
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or as the
      <a href="/wiki/Rooster" title="Rooster">
       rooster
      </a>
      (cock) serves as a tangible vessel of Christ as in the gospel of Matthew, Mark and Luke in the New Testament with Christ speaking through the cock;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
       <a href="#cite_note-217">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        217
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or as attendants, as in the case of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugin_and_Munin" title="Hugin and Munin">
       Hugin and Munin
      </a>
      , two
      <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">
       common ravens
      </a>
      who whispered news into the ears of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_god" title="Norse god">
       Norse god
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">
       Odin
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
       <a href="#cite_note-218">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        218
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In several civilizations of
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Italy" title="History of Italy">
       ancient Italy
      </a>
      , particularly
      <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_mythology" title="Etruscan mythology">
       Etruscan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">
       Roman
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religion_in_Ancient_Rome" title="Religion in Ancient Rome">
       religion
      </a>
      , priests were involved in
      <a href="/wiki/Augur" title="Augur">
       augury
      </a>
      , or interpreting the words of birds while the "auspex" (from which the word "auspicious" is derived) watched their activities to foretell events.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
       <a href="#cite_note-219">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        219
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They may also serve as
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_symbolism" title="Religious symbolism">
       religious symbols
      </a>
      , as when
      <a href="/wiki/Jonah" title="Jonah">
       Jonah
      </a>
      (Hebrew:
      <b>
       
      </b>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dove" title="Dove">
       dove
      </a>
      ) embodied the fright, passivity, mourning, and beauty traditionally associated with doves.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220">
       <a href="#cite_note-220">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        220
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Birds have themselves been deified, as in the case of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_peacock" title="Common peacock">
       common peacock
      </a>
      , which is perceived as Mother Earth by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dravidian_people" title="Dravidian people">
       Dravidians
      </a>
      of India.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221">
       <a href="#cite_note-221">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        221
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some birds have also been perceived as monsters, including the mythological
      <a href="/wiki/Roc_(mythology)" title="Roc (mythology)">
       Roc
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Mori people">
       Mori
      </a>
      's legendary
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Poukai" title="Poukai">
        Poukai
       </a>
      </i>
      , a giant bird capable of snatching humans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222">
       <a href="#cite_note-222">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        222
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:17.6-24-1974-Cuerda-seca-flisepanel.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1911" data-file-width="3489" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/17.6-24-1974-Cuerda-seca-flisepanel.jpg/220px-17.6-24-1974-Cuerda-seca-flisepanel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/17.6-24-1974-Cuerda-seca-flisepanel.jpg/330px-17.6-24-1974-Cuerda-seca-flisepanel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/17.6-24-1974-Cuerda-seca-flisepanel.jpg/440px-17.6-24-1974-Cuerda-seca-flisepanel.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:17.6-24-1974-Cuerda-seca-flisepanel.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        painted
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiles" title="Tiles">
         tiles
        </a>
        with design of birds from
        <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">
         Qajar dynasty
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Birds have been featured in culture and art since prehistoric times, when they were represented in early
      <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">
       cave paintings
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223">
       <a href="#cite_note-223">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        223
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Birds were later used in religious or symbolic art and design, such as the magnificent
      <a href="/wiki/Peacock_Throne" title="Peacock Throne">
       Peacock Throne
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mughal_era" title="Mughal era">
       Mughal
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">
       Persian
      </a>
      emperors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224">
       <a href="#cite_note-224">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        224
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With the advent of scientific interest in birds, many paintings of birds were commissioned for books. Among the most famous of these bird artists was
      <a href="/wiki/John_James_Audubon" title="John James Audubon">
       John James Audubon
      </a>
      , whose paintings of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_North_American_birds" title="List of North American birds">
       North American birds
      </a>
      were a great commercial success in Europe and who later lent his name to the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Audubon_Society" title="National Audubon Society">
       National Audubon Society
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225">
       <a href="#cite_note-225">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        225
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Birds are also important figures in poetry; for example,
      <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">
       Homer
      </a>
      incorporated
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nightingale" title="Nightingale">
       nightingales
      </a>
      into his
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">
        Odyssey
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Catullus" title="Catullus">
       Catullus
      </a>
      used a
      <a href="/wiki/Sparrow" title="Sparrow">
       sparrow
      </a>
      as an erotic symbol in his
      <a href="/wiki/Catullus_2" title="Catullus 2">
       Catullus 2
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226">
       <a href="#cite_note-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        226
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The relationship between an
      <a href="/wiki/Albatross" title="Albatross">
       albatross
      </a>
      and a sailor is the central theme of
      <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">
       Samuel Taylor Coleridge
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/The_Rime_of_the_Ancient_Mariner" title="The Rime of the Ancient Mariner">
       The Rime of the Ancient Mariner
      </a>
      , which led to the use of the
      <a href="/wiki/Albatross_(metaphor)" title="Albatross (metaphor)">
       term as a metaphor for a 'burden'
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227">
       <a href="#cite_note-227">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        227
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other
      <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
       English
      </a>
      metaphors derive from birds;
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vulture_fund" title="Vulture fund">
       vulture funds
      </a>
      and vulture investors, for instance, take their name from the scavenging vulture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228">
       <a href="#cite_note-228">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        228
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Perceptions of various bird species often vary across cultures.
      <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">
       Owls
      </a>
      are associated with bad luck,
      <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft" title="Witchcraft">
       witchcraft
      </a>
      , and death in parts of Africa,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229">
       <a href="#cite_note-229">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        229
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but are regarded as wise across much of Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230">
       <a href="#cite_note-230">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        230
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Hoopoe" title="Hoopoe">
       Hoopoes
      </a>
      were considered sacred in
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">
       Ancient Egypt
      </a>
      and symbols of virtue in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persia
      </a>
      , but were thought of as thieves across much of Europe and harbingers of war in
      <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">
       Scandinavia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231">
       <a href="#cite_note-231">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        231
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In religious images preserved from the Inca and Tiwanaku empires, birds are depicted in the process of transgressing boundaries between earthly and underground spiritual realms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2011Tiwanaku_232-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith2011Tiwanaku-232">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        232
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Indigenous peoples of the central Andes maintain legends of birds passing to and from metaphysical worlds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2011Tiwanaku_232-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith2011Tiwanaku-232">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        232
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The mythical chullumpi bird is said to mark the existence of a portal between such worlds, and to transform itself into a llama.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2011Tiwanaku_232-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith2011Tiwanaku-232">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        232
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dransart2002Earth_233-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dransart2002Earth-233">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        233
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">
       Conservation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:California-Condor3-Szmurlo_edit.jpg">
        <img alt="Large black bird with featherless head and hooked bill" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2222" data-file-width="1752" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/California-Condor3-Szmurlo_edit.jpg/170px-California-Condor3-Szmurlo_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/California-Condor3-Szmurlo_edit.jpg/255px-California-Condor3-Szmurlo_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/California-Condor3-Szmurlo_edit.jpg/340px-California-Condor3-Szmurlo_edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:California-Condor3-Szmurlo_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/California_condor" title="California condor">
         California condor
        </a>
        once numbered only 22 birds, but conservation measures have raised that to over 300 today.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_conservation" title="Bird conservation">
       Bird conservation
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Late_Quaternary_prehistoric_birds" title="Late Quaternary prehistoric birds">
       Late Quaternary prehistoric birds
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_birds" title="List of extinct birds">
       List of extinct birds
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Raptor_conservation" title="Raptor conservation">
       Raptor conservation
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Though human activities have allowed the expansion of a few species, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Barn_swallow" title="Barn swallow">
       barn swallow
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_starling" title="European starling">
       European starling
      </a>
      , they have caused population decreases or
      <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">
       extinction
      </a>
      in many other species. Over a hundred bird species have gone extinct in historical times,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234">
       <a href="#cite_note-234">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        234
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although the most dramatic human-caused avian extinctions, eradicating an estimated 7501800 species, occurred during the human colonisation of
      <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">
       Melanesian
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">
       Polynesian
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">
       Micronesian
      </a>
      islands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235">
       <a href="#cite_note-235">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        235
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many bird populations are declining worldwide, with 1,227 species listed as
      <a href="/wiki/Threatened_species" title="Threatened species">
       threatened
      </a>
      by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birdlife_International" title="Birdlife International">
       Birdlife International
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN">
       IUCN
      </a>
      in 2009.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236">
       <a href="#cite_note-236">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        236
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237">
       <a href="#cite_note-237">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        237
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The most commonly cited human threat to birds is
      <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">
       habitat loss
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238">
       <a href="#cite_note-238">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        238
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other threats include overhunting, accidental mortality due to
      <a href="/wiki/Bird_strike" title="Bird strike">
       structural collisions
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-line_fishing" title="Long-line fishing">
       long-line fishing
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Bycatch" title="Bycatch">
       bycatch
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239">
       <a href="#cite_note-239">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        239
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      pollution (including
      <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">
       oil spills
      </a>
      and pesticide use),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240">
       <a href="#cite_note-240">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        240
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      competition and predation from nonnative
      <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">
       invasive species
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241">
       <a href="#cite_note-241">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        241
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and climate change.
     </p>
     <p>
      Governments and
      <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">
       conservation
      </a>
      groups work to protect birds, either by passing laws that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_conservation" title="In-situ conservation">
       preserve
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological_restoration" title="Ecological restoration">
       restore
      </a>
      bird habitat or by establishing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ex-situ_conservation" title="Ex-situ conservation">
       captive populations
      </a>
      for reintroductions. Such projects have produced some successes; one study estimated that conservation efforts saved 16 species of bird that would otherwise have gone extinct between 1994 and 2004, including the
      <a href="/wiki/California_condor" title="California condor">
       California condor
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_parakeet" title="Norfolk parakeet">
       Norfolk parakeet
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242">
       <a href="#cite_note-242">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        242
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
       See also
      </span>
     </h2>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Animal_track" title="Animal track">
        Animal track
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABird&amp;rft.atitle=Bird+Mortality+Following+DDT+Spray+for+Dutch+Elm+Disease&amp;rft.aufirst=D&amp;rft.aulast=Wurster&amp;rft.au=Strickland%2C+W&amp;rft.au=Wurster%2C+C&amp;rft.au=Wurster%2C+D&amp;rft.date=1965&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F1934880&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.jtitle=Ecology&amp;rft.pages=488-99&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=46">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         ;
         <span class="citation journal">
          Wurster, C. F.; Wurster, D. H.; Strickland, W. N. (1965). "Bird Mortality after Spraying for Dutch Elm Disease with DDT".

          <td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2">
           <div>
            <ul>
             <li>
              subclasses of
              <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">
               Sarcopterygii
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              orders of class
              <i>
               <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">
                Reptilia
                <small>
                 (reptiles)
                </small>
               </a>
              </i>
             </li>
             <li>
              traditionally placed in
              <a href="/wiki/Anapsid" title="Anapsid">
               Anapsida
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              <i>
               italic
              </i>
              are paraphyletic groups
             </li>
            </ul>
           </div>
          </td>
